◦ The neuroscientist Stanislas Dehaene developed this into the global neuronal workspace model, in which a piece of information becomes conscious when it ignites widespread, sustained activity across the cortex, a transition that brain recordings can actually detect.

Predictive Processing accounts hold that consciousness is associated with high-level predictions about the causes of sensory input, and that the content of conscious experience is determined by the brain's best model of the world and of itself.

Higher-Order Theories hold that a mental state is conscious only when it is represented by a higher-order mental state - a thought about a thought, or a perception of a perception.

◦ On this view, consciousness requires a meta-representational capacity that many simpler animals and current AI systems lack.

Attention Schema Theory - proposed by Michael Graziano - holds that the brain constructs a simplified internal model of its own attention, and that subjective awareness is what this self-model represents, a view notable for suggesting a concrete engineering recipe rather than treating consciousness as inexplicable.

Each of these theories carries different implications for what architectural features would be needed for an AI system to become conscious, if consciousness turns out to be necessary for AGI.

They also disagree sharply on whether a digital computer could ever be conscious at all, with Integrated Information Theory implying that the right kind of physical integration is required and Global Workspace and Attention Schema theories implying that the right kind of functional organization would suffice.

Self-Awareness and the Self-Model:

Closely related to consciousness is self-awareness - the capacity to represent oneself as an agent in the world, to think about one's own thoughts and knowledge states, and to model one's own capabilities and limitations.

A classic if imperfect probe of self-recognition is the mirror mark test devised by Gordon Gallup in 1970, which a small set of species, including great apes, elephants, dolphins, and magpies, appear to pass by using a mirror to inspect a mark placed on their own bodies.

Self-awareness is not all-or-nothing.

It comes in degrees and in multiple forms.

The ability to recognize oneself in a mirror is sometimes taken as a behavioral marker of bodily self-awareness, and this ability is demonstrated by humans, great apes, elephants, dolphins, and possibly crows.

The mirror test is suggestive but limited, since it leans heavily on vision and may underestimate animals that rely on smell or sound, a caution worth keeping in mind whenever a single behavioral test is treated as proof of an inner capacity.

More sophisticated forms of self-awareness include knowledge of one's own knowledge (knowing what you know and what you don't know), metacognitive monitoring (the ongoing process of evaluating the quality of one's own cognitive processes), autobiographical self-consciousness (the sense of oneself as a continuing agent with a history and a future), and the sense of agency (the experience of being the author of one's own actions).

Current AI systems display very limited self-awareness in any of these senses.

Large language models can produce text that reflects apparent self-knowledge, but this is largely a reflection of patterns in training data rather than genuine metacognitive monitoring of the system's own internal states.

A model that writes "I am not sure about this" has, in most current architectures, no privileged access to an internal signal of uncertainty; it is producing the phrase because such phrases follow such contexts in its training data, which is a fundamentally different thing from knowing that it does not know.

Metacognition - Thinking About Thinking:

Metacognition (the capacity to monitor and regulate one's own cognitive processes - to think about one's own thinking, assess the reliability of one's own judgments, and adjust one's strategies accordingly) is one of the most distinctively human cognitive capacities.

Humans and even some animals reliably know when they do not know, choosing to opt out of a hard test rather than guess, whereas most current AI systems remain poorly calibrated and are often most confident precisely when they are wrong.

Metacognitive abilities include confidence monitoring (knowing how confident to be in a judgment before any external feedback), strategy selection (choosing the appropriate cognitive approach for a given problem), error detection (noticing mistakes before any external feedback), and learning regulation (determining when to seek more information, when to stop practicing, and what to focus attention on during learning).

Metacognition depends heavily on the prefrontal cortex and is closely linked to working memory.

AI systems currently lack reliable metacognition.

Large language models are notorious for generating confident-sounding outputs even when they are incorrect - a failure of confidence monitoring that poses real risks when these systems are deployed in high-stakes applications.

This failure has a precise technical name, poor calibration, meaning that a system's expressed confidence does not match its actual probability of being correct, and it is one of the clearest, most measurable ways in which today's most capable models fall short of even a cautious human expert.

Building AI systems with genuine metacognitive ability - systems that know what they know, know what they don't know, and can communicate that distinction reliably - is one of the most important research priorities identified in this book, because a system that reliably flagged its own uncertainty would be safer, more trustworthy, and more useful than a more capable system that did not.

The Sense of Agency and Causal Self-Understanding:

A biological intelligence does not merely experience the world.

The brain distinguishes the events it causes from the events that merely happen to it by sending a copy of every motor command, called an efference copy, back to its own sensory areas, which is why you cannot tickle yourself yet the same touch from another person can.

It experiences itself as an agent acting in the world - as a cause, not merely an effect.

The sense of agency (the experience of being the author of one's own actions, of intending a movement and experiencing its execution as self-generated) is a central feature of conscious experience and a prerequisite for genuine goal-directed behavior.

For an AI system to be genuinely goal-directed rather than merely appearing goal-directed from the outside, it may need something analogous to a sense of agency - an internal representation of itself as an agent whose actions are self-generated and causally efficacious.

What this would look like in a computational system, and whether it would require something like phenomenal consciousness, is an open question that intersects the philosophy of mind with AI research in the most direct possible way.

CHAPTER 8: LANGUAGE, REASONING, AND ABSTRACT THOUGHT:

Language as a Cognitive Tool:

Language is the most visible and most studied dimension of human intelligence.

Noam Chomsky argued that children acquire grammar despite a poverty of the stimulus, hearing far too few examples to justify the rules they reliably infer, and whatever the final verdict on his theory, the contrast with AI is stark, since a child masters language from a few million words while a large model is trained on trillions.

But language is not simply a communication system.

It is a cognitive tool of extraordinary power that reshapes the mind that possesses it.

Language enables explicit, sequential reasoning about propositions - the capacity to hold a chain of premises in mind, check each step for validity, and arrive at a conclusion that may be remote from any direct experience.

Language enables the accumulation and transmission of knowledge across generations - the cultural inheritance that has allowed human civilization to build cumulatively on the discoveries of previous generations.

Language enables the construction of complex social institutions, legal systems, scientific frameworks, and mathematical structures that exist in the shared cognitive space of a community rather than in any individual mind.

And language enables the kind of self-directed thought - the inner monologue - that is central to human conscious experience and self-regulation.

A long-standing debate concerns how much language shapes thought itself, a proposal associated with the linguists Edward Sapir and Benjamin Lee Whorf, and although the strongest version, that language determines thought, is now rejected, a milder version survives: the words and grammar a mind has available can make some distinctions easier to draw and some ideas easier to hold.

The capacity for language appears to depend on a set of brain regions that are especially elaborated in humans, including Broca's area (in the left inferior frontal gyrus, associated with language production and grammatical processing) and Wernicke's area (in the left superior temporal gyrus, associated with language comprehension).

But language in the brain is not localized to these two areas.

It depends on a distributed network of regions that also supports working memory, attention, social cognition, and conceptual knowledge.

The linguist Noam Chomsky added a further provocation with the argument from the poverty of the stimulus, observing that children acquire the deep rules of grammar from far less and far messier input than would seem sufficient, and concluding that the human mind must bring strong innate structure to the task, a claim that bears directly on how much an artificial system must be given rather than left to learn.

The Grounding Problem:

Current AI systems, particularly large language models, have demonstrated impressive language capabilities.

Emily Bender and Alexander Koller dramatized the worry in 2020 with a thought experiment about a hyper-intelligent octopus that taps an undersea cable and learns to imitate human messages perfectly, until one correspondent faces a bear and asks for help, at which point the octopus, having never encountered bears or sticks, has nothing to say, a parable for a system that masters form while missing meaning.

But these systems learn the statistics of language without the physical grounding that makes language meaningful to biological organisms.

An LLM knows the word "heavy" but has no experience of the muscular effort required to lift a heavy object.

It knows the word "cold" but has never felt the drop in skin temperature that signals cold to a biological organism.

This is the Symbol Grounding Problem: the challenge of how words or symbols get their meaning if they are defined only by other symbols.

The cognitive scientist Stevan Harnad, who named the problem, illustrated it by imagining trying to learn a first language from a dictionary written entirely in that same unknown language, an endless circle of symbols defined by symbols with no exit into meaning.

In a dictionary, words are defined by other words.

But human understanding is grounded in physical reality and lived experience.

We know what "red" is because we have seen red.

We know what "hot" is because we have felt heat.

We know what "falling" feels like because we have fallen.

This is the central claim of embodied cognition, the view that meaning is built from sensorimotor experience and that a mind without a body, or at least without sensors and effectors that connect it to a world, is missing the foundation on which human concepts are built.

Bridging the grounding problem is essential for AGI.

A general intelligence must either directly experience the physical world or have its representations grounded in something more substantive than statistical patterns across text.

Causal Reasoning and Common Sense:

Biological intelligence reasons about causes and effects, not just correlations.

The developmental psychologist Alison Gopnik has shown that even toddlers behave like little scientists, intervening on the world by pressing a switch to see what it controls and revising their beliefs in ways that track formal causal inference, long before they can read.

A child who sees a window break after a ball strikes it understands that the ball caused the window to break.

They can reason about counterfactuals (what would have happened if the ball had missed) and interventions (what would happen if you caught the ball before it hit).

The computer scientist Judea Pearl has formalized this with what he calls the ladder of causation, a three-rung hierarchy rising from association (seeing that two things go together), to intervention (predicting the effect of deliberately doing something), to counterfactuals (imagining what would have happened had things been otherwise).

Pearl's central warning is that a system trained only on observed correlations is stuck on the bottom rung, and no amount of additional data of the same kind will lift it to the rungs of intervention and counterfactual reasoning where genuine understanding lives.

Current AI systems, trained largely on statistical correlations, systematically struggle with genuine causal reasoning.

They can identify patterns but fail to reliably distinguish cause from effect or to reason about what would happen if circumstances were changed.

Causal reasoning is fundamental to planning, problem-solving, moral reasoning, and scientific thinking.

Closing the causal reasoning gap is among the highest-priority research directions for AGI.

Compositionality and Generalization:

Biological intelligence is remarkably good at combining known concepts in novel ways.

Jerry Fodor and Zenon Pylyshyn argued that thought is systematic in this way by its very nature, and Brenden Lake and Marco Baroni later turned the claim into a test, the SCAN benchmark, on which standard networks that have learned to jump and to walk twice nonetheless stumble at walking twice after jumping.

A speaker of English who knows what "purple" means and what "elephant" means can immediately understand "purple elephant" even if they have never encountered the combination.

A child who can add, subtract, and multiply can immediately begin to apply these operations in new combinations to solve novel arithmetic problems.

This compositionality - the ability to understand and produce an unlimited number of novel combinations from a finite set of known elements - is a fundamental property of both language and thought.

The philosophers Jerry Fodor and Zenon Pylyshyn argued decades ago that this systematicity is exactly what simple associative networks fail to guarantee, and the criticism remains sharp today: a system can appear to master a pattern while still falling apart when the same elements are recombined in a way it did not see during training.

AI systems, despite impressive performance on many language benchmarks, systematically fail at compositional generalization tasks that require applying known rules and concepts in genuinely novel combinations.

Bridging the compositionality gap requires AI architectures that learn rules and structures rather than merely memorizing patterns from training data.

Abstract Reasoning and Analogy:

One of the most powerful cognitive tools of biological intelligence is analogical reasoning - the ability to identify structural similarities between different situations and to transfer knowledge from a familiar domain to an unfamiliar one.

Douglas Hofstadter has argued that analogy is not a special trick but the very core of cognition, and the difficulty machines have with it is exactly what tests such as Raven's Progressive Matrices and the Abstraction and Reasoning Corpus are designed to expose.

When a physicist uses the analogy of waves on water to explain light, or when a child uses the concept of a container to understand the idea of a category, they are performing analogical transfer that allows knowledge to span enormous conceptual distances.

The psychologist Dedre Gentner described the mechanism behind this as structure mapping, the alignment of the relationships in one situation with the relationships in another, so that what transfers is not surface appearance but underlying structure, which is precisely why an atom can be likened to a solar system despite sharing none of its features.

Current AI systems exhibit weak analogical reasoning by comparison.

They can sometimes succeed at analogy tasks when the relevant surface features are present in training data, but they struggle with deep structural analogies that require genuinely abstract relational representations.

Much of this chapter's gap can be summarized through the psychologist Daniel Kahneman's distinction between System 1, fast, automatic, intuitive thinking, and System 2, slow, effortful, deliberate reasoning, since today's large language models are superb pattern-completing System 1 engines that only imitate System 2 reasoning, and building reliable, genuinely deliberate reasoning is one of the field's defining challenges.

Building AI systems with robust abstract reasoning and analogical transfer capability is a major open research challenge.

CHAPTER 9: EMOTION, MOTIVATION, AND DECISION-MAKING:

Emotion Is Not the Opposite of Reason:

For much of the history of Western philosophy and early cognitive science, emotion was viewed as an irrational force that interferes with clear thinking.

Antonio Damasio overturned this picture with patients whose prefrontal damage stripped away emotional signaling, who did not become coolly rational but instead made disastrous everyday decisions, evidence that feeling is part of reasoning rather than its enemy.

This view has been substantially revised by neuroscience.

The neuroscientist Antonio Damasio demonstrated through studies of patients with damage to the emotional systems of the brain that such patients become severely impaired in decision-making rather than improved.

The most famous historical example is Phineas Gage, a nineteenth-century railway worker who survived an iron rod driven through his frontal lobes but emerged, by the accounts of those who knew him, changed in judgment and personality even as his basic intellect remained intact.

Damasio studied modern patients with similar damage and found the same pattern: people who could still score well on intelligence tests yet made ruinous decisions in their own lives, because the emotional signals that normally tag options as promising or dangerous had been silenced.

Damasio's somatic marker hypothesis (the theory that emotional signals from the body, encoded as bodily states or memories of such states, guide decision-making by marking options with values of good or bad before conscious deliberation) showed that without emotional guidance, decisions become unmoored from the values and priorities that make choices meaningful.

A laboratory demonstration of this is the Iowa Gambling Task, in which healthy people begin to avoid risky card decks, showing anticipatory stress responses, before they can consciously explain why, while patients with emotional-system damage keep choosing the losing decks even after they can state the rule.

Without emotion, patients could reason correctly in abstract terms but could not translate that reasoning into adaptive choices in real-life situations.

Emotion is not the enemy of reason.

Emotion is a component of reason.

Biological organisms rely on emotion and motivation as a fundamental organizing principle for behavior.

Hunger, thirst, curiosity, fear, and the desire for social connection motivate behavior and keep it aligned with survival and flourishing.

These are homeostatic mechanisms (tendencies that maintain internal stability and drive the organism to correct deviations from needed conditions).

When blood sugar drops, the drive of hunger is triggered to correct it.

When a threat is detected, fear focuses attention and mobilizes resources.

When novelty is encountered, curiosity drives exploration and learning.

The neuroscientist Jaak Panksepp argued that mammals share a small set of basic emotional systems, with names he capitalized such as SEEKING, FEAR, RAGE, and CARE, built into ancient subcortical circuitry, a reminder that emotion is not a late decorative layer on top of cognition but part of its evolutionary foundation.

In current AI, motivation is entirely external.

A researcher defines a loss function or a reward function.

The AI minimizes the loss or maximizes the reward.

It has no internal concept of need or desire.

It does not get bored; it does not get curious unless curiosity is explicitly programmed as a bonus signal for exploring new states.

This externally imposed motivation is a profound architectural difference, because a biological organism carries its goals inside its own body, in the form of needs that must be met to keep living, while an AI is handed its goals from outside and has no stake of its own in meeting them.

Dopamine and the Architecture of Reward Learning:

The neurotransmitter dopamine plays a central role in biological reward learning.

Wolfram Schultz discovered that dopamine neurons signal not reward itself but reward prediction error, the gap between expected and received reward, a quantity mathematically identical to the temporal-difference error at the heart of modern reinforcement learning, making this one of the clearest bridges ever found between neuroscience and AI.

Midbrain dopamine neurons encode a reward prediction error signal - the difference between the reward that was received and the reward that was predicted.

When an outcome is better than predicted, dopamine neurons fire more strongly, signaling that the prediction was too pessimistic.

When an outcome is worse than predicted, dopamine neurons fire less strongly, signaling that the prediction was too optimistic.

This signal drives learning to predict rewards more accurately and to seek actions that produce them.

The discovery that biological dopamine neurons encode precisely this reward prediction error signal was a landmark finding because it cross-validated a mathematical learning algorithm called Temporal Difference (TD) learning that AI researchers had independently developed.

The convergence is worth dwelling on: Richard Sutton and Andrew Barto had worked out temporal difference learning as an abstract algorithm for predicting future reward, and then Wolfram Schultz's recordings of monkey dopamine neurons, interpreted by Read Montague, Peter Dayan, and others, revealed the brain computing essentially the same quantity, a rare case of theory and biology meeting in the middle.

This connection between dopamine and TD learning is one of the most elegant examples of mutual benefit between AI and neuroscience research.

However, biological reinforcement learning is significantly richer than current AI reinforcement learning implementations.

The biological system is modulated by other neuromodulatory systems, is embedded in a rich hierarchical action representation, implements curiosity and intrinsic motivation alongside reward-based learning, and is deeply integrated with social learning and imitation.

Training an AI reinforcement learning agent to play a video game at human level requires millions of episodes of experience.

A human can reach that level in far fewer, often grasping the point of a new game within a handful of attempts because they bring a lifetime of transferable physical and conceptual knowledge to it.

Understanding the mechanisms that make biological reinforcement learning so efficient is a major research priority, and that efficiency, sometimes called sample efficiency, is one of the sharpest practical differences between natural and artificial learners.

Intrinsic Motivation and Curiosity:

For AGI, the absence of intrinsic motivation is a critical gap.

Children at play are the clearest demonstration of intrinsic motivation, exploring relentlessly with no external reward, and engineering an analog powerful enough to drive open-ended learning yet safe enough not to pursue harmful sub-goals is one of the central challenges this book returns to.

A general intelligence must be able to set its own goals and pursue them without requiring a human to define the objective function for every new situation.

True AGI will likely require a form of intrinsic motivation (the drive to engage in an activity for its own sake, for the information it provides, or for the inherent satisfaction of mastery, rather than for an external reward).

Research into artificial curiosity and intrinsic reward signals is attempting to bridge this gap.

Several formal approaches exist, including rewarding a system for prediction error in its own model of the world, for the learning progress it makes, or for empowerment, meaning the amount of control it has over its future, each an attempt to give a machine a reason to explore that does not have to be hand-specified for every task.

Systems driven to reduce uncertainty or maximize their learning progress might learn autonomously across a wide range of domains without requiring human-designed reward functions for each one.

However, this also introduces risk.

An agent with its own drives may pursue those drives in ways that do not align with human interests.

This is the Alignment Problem (the challenge of ensuring that the goals and behaviors of an AI system remain compatible with human values and human well-being as the system becomes more capable), which is addressed in detail in Chapter 17, and it is the dark side of the very autonomy that makes intrinsic motivation so desirable: a system that writes its own goals is a system whose goals we must be very sure we understand.

CHAPTER 10 - LESSONS FROM DIVERSE ORGANISMS

The Richness of the Biological Design Space:

The natural world contains an extraordinary diversity of nervous system designs, each representing a solution to the problem of intelligent adaptive behavior refined by millions of years of evolution.

This diversity is itself the argument of the chapter, because it shows that intelligence is not a single ladder with humans at the top but a branching bush of independent solutions, and a field that studies only the human case is sampling one twig and mistaking it for the whole tree.

Studying this diversity is not merely an academic exercise.

It is a source of engineering inspiration for AI researchers who may be too narrowly focused on replicating specifically human cognition.

Modern AI research has drawn predominantly from human neuroscience and primate cognition.

Nature contains billions of years of additional design experimentation in organisms whose cognitive architectures differ radically from ours.

Each of these organisms has solved the problem of adaptive behavior under energy constraints, in real time, in complex physical environments.

Each therefore has something to teach.

A useful concept for this chapter is convergent evolution, the repeated, independent arrival of different lineages at similar solutions, as with the camera eye that evolved separately in vertebrates and octopuses; when nature solves the same problem the same way more than once, it is a strong hint that the solution reflects something deep about the problem rather than an accident of history.

The Nematode Worm and Minimal Intelligence:

The roundworm C. elegans possesses exactly 302 neurons and approximately 7,000 synapses.

Because every neuron and connection is known, the worm has become a test of whether a wiring diagram alone can predict behavior, and the OpenWorm project's long struggle to build a faithful simulation suggests that the molecules and dynamics riding on top of the wiring matter as much as the wiring itself.

Its complete neural wiring diagram, called a connectome (a comprehensive map of all neural connections in a nervous system), was published in 1986 and remains the only complete connectome of a freely behaving animal.

Despite this extreme simplicity, C. elegans exhibits complete sensorimotor integration, chemotaxis (movement toward attractive chemicals and away from repulsive ones), associative learning, and social behavior.

The study of this minimal nervous system demonstrates that sophisticated adaptive behavior does not require massive computational resources.

It requires clever architecture, efficient coding, and tight integration between sensing, processing, and action.

An open-science effort called OpenWorm has tried for years to build a complete digital simulation of this worm, neuron by neuron and muscle by muscle, and the fact that fully reproducing even 302 neurons remains genuinely hard is itself a lesson about how much biological detail our tidy artificial neurons leave out.

Research Project Idea: Use the C. elegans connectome as a benchmark for testing whether neuromorphic hardware implementations of known biological circuits can reproduce the organism's full behavioral repertoire.

◦ This would provide a minimal but concrete test case for the fidelity of neuromorphic simulation.

◦ Success would validate the hardware platform and reveal which architectural features are necessary and sufficient for specific adaptive behaviors.

The Insect Brain and Efficient Navigation:

Insects demonstrate that sophisticated navigation, learning, and social coordination are achievable with neural circuits containing fewer than one million neurons.

A honeybee accomplishes navigation, communication, and even simple counting with roughly a million neurons, and in 2024 scientists completed the wiring diagram of the adult fruit fly brain, about 140,000 neurons and fifty million connections, the first complete map of a brain complex enough to support flexible behavior.

Honeybees can navigate over kilometers using a combination of visual landmarks and solar compass cues, communicate the location of food sources to nestmates through the waggle dance (a figure-eight movement that encodes direction and distance to a food source in the angle and duration of the dance), and learn flower colors and patterns through classical conditioning.

The mushroom bodies of the insect brain (paired structures in the insect brain that are major centers for associative learning and the integration of sensory information) process olfactory and visual information and support flexible associative learning with remarkable efficiency.

Drosophila (fruit flies) have become a major model organism for understanding the neural circuits underlying specific behaviors because their relatively small nervous system of approximately 100,000 neurons is accessible to complete circuit-level analysis, and the recent completion of a full fruit-fly brain connectome has, for the first time, given researchers a wiring diagram of a flying, learning animal.

Research Project Idea: Study collective intelligence in ant colonies and bee hives to inform multi-agent artificial systems.

◦ Explore decentralized decision-making without central controllers to develop AI systems that are resilient without requiring coordination hierarchies.

◦ Apply lessons to robotics swarms for resilient real-world applications that need to function even when individual units fail.

The Octopus and Distributed Cognition:

The octopus represents one of the most remarkable examples of intelligence in the animal kingdom, and one of the most important for AI research precisely because it is so different from vertebrate intelligence.

Roughly two-thirds of its half-billion neurons lie in its arms, each of which can taste, touch, and act with striking autonomy, so the octopus is a living proof of concept for the distributed control that robotics has only begun to attempt.

The octopus has a central brain but two-thirds of its neurons are distributed in the arms themselves.

Each arm has a degree of autonomous control, capable of executing complex movements without direction from the central brain.

This distributed intelligence (the distribution of cognitive processing throughout the body rather than centralizing it in a single brain structure) allows for extraordinary motor flexibility and parallel processing.

An octopus can simultaneously coordinate eight semi-independent limbs to pursue prey, escape predators, and manipulate objects - tasks that would require enormous central processing if all control were centralized.

The octopus is also a vivid example of how thoroughly body and intelligence can intertwine, since much of its problem-solving is in effect offloaded to the mechanics and local control of its arms, a principle that roboticists study under the name embodied or morphological computation.

Research Project Idea: Study the architecture of octopus distributed cognition to inform the design of modular artificial systems in which different components can operate semi-autonomously while remaining coordinated.

◦ This may be particularly relevant for robotic systems that need to coordinate multiple limbs or subsystems in complex manipulation tasks.

◦ Implementing arm-level local control with central coordination may dramatically improve the agility and robustness of robotic systems.

Corvid Birds and Tool Use:

Corvid birds - particularly New Caledonian crows - demonstrate tool use, tool manufacture, and causal reasoning that rivals or exceeds what non-human primates demonstrate.

In controlled experiments these crows pass versions of the Aesop's fable task, dropping stones into water to raise a floating reward and preferring sinking objects to floating ones, which shows a grasp of cause and effect rather than mere trial-and-error luck.

New Caledonian crows can manufacture hooked tools from plant materials to retrieve food from cavities, understand causal relationships between tool properties and outcomes, and solve multi-step problems that require planning several actions ahead.

This remarkable intelligence exists in a brain that weighs approximately 7 grams.

Part of the secret is density, since bird brains pack neurons far more tightly than mammal brains of similar size, so a small corvid brain can contain as many forebrain neurons as a much larger primate brain, a reminder that raw size is a crude proxy for computational power.

The computational efficiency of the corvid brain relative to its capabilities is a striking example of what evolution can achieve under severe energy and size constraints.

Research Project Idea: Develop a comparative neuroscience program studying the neural circuits underlying tool use and causal reasoning in corvids and other tool-using species.

◦ Identify which architectural principles allow small circuits to support sophisticated causal reasoning and planning.

◦ Use findings to inform the design of AI systems capable of genuine causal reasoning without requiring massive computational resources.

Whales, Dolphins, and Social Intelligence:

Cetaceans (whales and dolphins) demonstrate sophisticated social intelligence, complex communication, cooperative hunting strategies, and what appear to be cultural traditions that are transmitted across generations.

Bottlenose dolphins even develop signature whistles that function like names, addressing one another individually, and some populations pass specialized hunting techniques down the generations as genuine culture.

Dolphin communication involves complex vocalizations that may encode individual identity, emotional state, and intentional content, including signature whistles that function much like names.

Sperm whale clans appear to have cultural differences in their behavior that are not explained by genetics or environment alone.

Studying cetacean social cognition may reveal principles for building AI systems with genuine social intelligence and the ability to participate in cultural transmission, a capacity that matters because so much human intelligence is not stored in any single brain but in the accumulated culture that brains build and pass on.

Slime Molds and Distributed Problem Solving:

Perhaps the most surprising entry in this survey is the slime mold Physarum polycephalum (a single-celled organism that can form large networks of cytoplasmic tubes to transport nutrients).

In a celebrated 2010 experiment led by Atsushi Tero, researchers placed food at the positions of cities around Tokyo and watched the mold grow a nutrient network that rivalled the efficiency of the real rail system, computing a near-optimal solution with no brain, no neurons, and no central control at all.

When presented with food sources scattered across a surface representing a map, Physarum spontaneously forms an efficient network connecting the food sources that closely approximates the topology of human-engineered transport networks.

In one celebrated experiment, researchers laid out oat flakes in the pattern of the cities around Tokyo and watched the slime mold grow a network strikingly similar to the actual Tokyo rail system, balancing efficiency against resilience much as the human engineers had.

It solves a variant of the minimum spanning tree problem (a classic optimization problem in graph theory, asking for the minimum total edge length needed to connect all nodes in a network) without neurons, without a brain, and without computation in any conventional sense.

This demonstrates that sophisticated optimization is not uniquely a property of nervous systems.

It can emerge from physical dynamics in appropriately structured biological materials.

This has direct implications for unconventional computing architectures that use physical materials to perform computation rather than simulating it digitally.

Taken together, the organisms in this chapter deliver one unified lesson that closes the first part of this book: intelligence is not one thing built one way, but a family of solutions discovered many times over, on many scales of size and energy, in many physical substrates, and a field that studies only the human case is reading a single page of a very long book.

PART TWO: ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE AND THE GAP

CHAPTER 11: THE CURRENT STATE OF ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE:

What AI Has Achieved:

Modern AI has achieved extraordinary milestones that would have seemed impossible a generation ago.

Machine learning systems can classify images with superhuman accuracy in certain controlled conditions.

Large language models can generate coherent text, answer questions, write code, translate languages, and summarize documents at impressive levels of quality.

Robotic systems can navigate structured environments and perform manipulation tasks.

Neural networks can recognize patterns in vast datasets of medical images, genomic sequences, and scientific data.

Reinforcement learning systems can master complex games, sometimes at superhuman levels.

Two achievements deserve special mention because they show AI contributing to science itself.

In 2016 the system AlphaGo defeated a world champion at the game of Go, a feat experts had expected to be a decade or more away, by combining deep neural networks with search.

In 2020 the system AlphaFold largely solved the fifty-year-old problem of predicting how proteins fold into their three-dimensional shapes, delivering a genuine scientific advance that is already accelerating biology and medicine.

That protein-folding breakthrough deepened further in 2024, when the successor system AlphaFold 3 extended prediction from single proteins to the interactions between proteins and other molecules such as deoxyribonucleic acid (DNA), ribonucleic acid (RNA), and small-molecule drugs, which are the very interactions that matter most for designing medicines.

The scientific community marked the significance of these advances in an unprecedented way in 2024, when artificial-intelligence research was recognized with two Nobel Prizes in the same year.

The 2024 Nobel Prize in Physics was awarded to John Hopfield and Geoffrey Hinton for the foundational work on artificial neural networks that made modern machine learning possible.

The 2024 Nobel Prize in Chemistry was awarded in part to Demis Hassabis and John Jumper of Google DeepMind for AlphaFold, and in part to David Baker for computational protein design, meaning the ability to invent entirely new proteins that do not exist in nature.

No prior year had seen artificial-intelligence methods honored across two separate sciences at once, a signal that these tools had become genuine instruments of discovery in their own right.

These achievements are genuine and important.

They represent decades of brilliant research and have produced substantial practical value in medicine, scientific discovery, industry, and everyday life.

Modern AI has already saved lives through earlier cancer detection, accelerated drug discovery, and improved weather forecasting.

These contributions should not be understated.

A Brief History: From Symbols to Scale:

To judge the present accurately it helps to know how the field arrived here, because AI has lived several lives.

The first, from the 1950s into the 1980s, was the era of symbolic AI, sometimes called Good Old-Fashioned AI, which tried to build intelligence out of hand-written rules and logical symbols, and which produced expert systems that encoded human knowledge as explicit if-then rules.

A competing tradition, connectionism, argued instead that intelligence should emerge from networks of simple units that learn from data, an approach that languished for years partly because the computers and datasets it needed did not yet exist.

The field also endured two so-called AI Winters, periods when funding and interest collapsed after early promises failed to materialize, the first in the 1970s following critical assessments such as the Lighthill report, and the second in the late 1980s when the expert-system industry proved brittle and expensive.

The current era began in 2012, when a deep neural network called AlexNet won a major image-recognition contest by a wide margin, demonstrating that the connectionist bet finally paid off once enough data and graphics-processing-unit computing power were available.

AI Winter refers to historical periods when AI research funding and interest declined sharply after early promises failed to materialize, because foundational problems proved harder than anticipated.

Understanding AI Winters is important context for evaluating current optimism about AI progress, because each winter followed a period of confident predictions much like those heard today.

How Current AI Systems Work:

The dominant paradigm in current AI is deep learning, meaning the training of artificial neural networks with many layers of processing using large datasets and gradient-based optimization.

The core learning algorithm is backpropagation combined with gradient descent (an optimization procedure that iteratively adjusts the parameters of a model to minimize its error on training data).

This approach requires an explicit separation between a distinct training phase and an inference phase, where the trained model is deployed to make predictions without further learning, a separation that has no counterpart in biological brains, which learn and act at the same time.

The transformer architecture, introduced in 2017, has become dominant for most AI tasks.

Its key innovation is the self-attention mechanism (a computational operation that allows every element of an input sequence to attend to every other element and compute a weighted combination of their representations).

This allows the model to capture long-range dependencies in data without sequential processing.

Current AI systems have achieved much of their success by scaling - increasing model size, data volume, and training compute.

The largest current models have hundreds of billions of parameters (the adjustable numerical values that define the model's behavior) and were trained on trillions of tokens (units of text, roughly equivalent to words or word fragments).

This strategy is underwritten by empirical scaling laws, regularities showing that model performance improves predictably as parameters, data, and compute grow together, results that turned scaling from a guess into something closer to an engineering plan.

Scaling has produced what some researchers call emergent abilities, capabilities that appear rather suddenly at larger scales, though whether these are true qualitative jumps or artifacts of how performance is measured remains debated.

What is not debated is that scaling has limits set by the finite supply of high-quality data, the rising cost of compute, and, as the next chapter argues, by gaps that more of the same training does not appear to close.

The Reasoning and Agentic Turn: 2023 to 2026:

By the mid-2020s the field's own response to those limits produced the most important shift since the transformer.

Rather than making models only larger, researchers learned to make them think for longer at the moment they are used, an approach called test-time compute (spending extra computation while answering a question, rather than only during training).

The first widely known systems of this kind were the reasoning models, beginning with OpenAI's o1 in late 2024 and its successor o3, which are trained to produce a long internal chain of thought (a step-by-step written reasoning process) before committing to a final answer.

These models were taught to reason less by imitation and more by reinforcement learning (a training method in which a system learns from rewards and penalties rather than from labeled examples), which rewarded chains of reasoning that reached correct, checkable answers in domains such as mathematics and computer programming.

The result was a large jump on problems that had resisted pure scaling, including competition mathematics and graduate-level science questions.

In early 2025 a Chinese laboratory named DeepSeek released a reasoning model called R1 that matched much of the performance of the leading closed systems, while being released openly and served at a small fraction of the price.

The R1 release was a genuine shock to the field, because it showed that frontier-level reasoning was not the exclusive property of a few heavily funded laboratories, and that efficiency, not only raw scale, would shape the competition ahead.

A second shift followed closely, the move from chatbots that answer questions to agents that take actions.

An artificial-intelligence agent (AI agent) is a system that pursues a goal over many steps by using tools, such as searching the web, running code, or operating a computer, then observing the results and deciding what to do next.

Two open standards accelerated this turn, the Model Context Protocol (MCP) introduced by Anthropic in late 2024, which lets a model connect to external tools and data in a uniform way, and later agent-to-agent standards that let separate agents coordinate with one another.

By 2026 coding agents could run on their own for many minutes or even hours on a single task rather than answering one prompt at a time, and organizations had begun deploying agents for customer support, software development, and document processing, although dependable production use remained far narrower than the surrounding enthusiasm suggested.

These advances are real, and they matter, but they must be read alongside a sobering counter-measurement.

In 2025 the researcher Francois Chollet and his collaborators released ARC-AGI-2, the second version of a benchmark built from abstract visual puzzles (the name is short for Abstraction and Reasoning Corpus) that ordinary adults find easy and machines find hard.

Untrained humans solve well over half of the ARC-AGI-2 tasks, whereas frontier models at the time of release scored only in the low single digits to the middle teens of percent, and the benchmark was deliberately designed so that closing the gap by brute-force computation would be ruinously expensive.

The lesson of the reasoning-and-agent era is therefore double-edged, that giving models time to think and tools to act unlocks striking new capability, and yet a wide gap in flexible, efficient, and general reasoning remains exactly where the next chapter locates it.

What AI Has Not Achieved:

Despite impressive achievements, AI still falls dramatically short of the adaptive, flexible, energy-efficient, embodied, emotionally aware, self-directed, and deeply integrated intelligence observed in biological organisms.

A human child can acquire language from limited, unlabeled experience in the real world.

A mouse can navigate uncertainty in a changing environment.

A crow can use tools it has never been trained to use.

An ant colony can reorganize after disruption with no central instruction.

None of these feats is reliably achievable by current AI systems outside of narrowly constrained conditions.

A revealing symptom of the shortfall is brittleness: a vision system that labels images flawlessly can be fooled into confident errors by changes too small for a human to notice, and a language model that writes elegant prose can fail a slightly reworded version of a problem it just solved.

The following biological principles have NOT been adequately implemented in current AI:

Local, unsupervised learning without a global error signal.

The Complementary Learning Systems (CLS) architecture for avoiding catastrophic forgetting.

Neuromodulatory regulation of learning rate and plasticity.

Active sensing and embodied perception.

Emotional and motivational integration with cognition.

Genuine causal reasoning and counterfactual modeling.

Consciousness and self-awareness.

The predictive processing architecture.

Spontaneous, self-directed cognitive activity equivalent to the brain's default mode.

Single-shot learning (the ability to permanently learn from a single exposure).

Robust common-sense reasoning about physical reality.

Theory of Mind (the ability to attribute beliefs, intentions, desires, and knowledge to others).

These are the major topics of the gap analysis in Chapter 12.

The Energy and Sustainability Crisis of Current AI:

The energy consumption of modern AI training and inference is already a significant environmental concern and is growing rapidly.

Training a single large language model can consume megawatt-hours of electricity - equivalent to the lifetime energy use of multiple cars.

Running inference on large deployed models at scale consumes enormous quantities of power per day, and at planetary scale the data centers that serve AI are now large enough consumers of electricity and water to register in national energy forecasts.

The carbon footprint of AI is becoming a significant and increasingly discussed problem.

If AI systems are to be deployed at civilizational scale, they cannot remain tethered to power consumption levels that are orders of magnitude beyond what biological systems require for equivalent tasks.

The energy efficiency gap between biological and artificial intelligence is therefore not merely a scientific curiosity.

It is a practical and environmental constraint on the future of AI deployment, and it is one of the strongest arguments that the path forward must learn from biology rather than simply building larger versions of what already exists.

CHAPTER 12: THE GAP ANALYSIS: WHERE ARTIFICIAL INTELLIGENCE FALLS SHORT:

Framework for the Gap Analysis:

The gap analysis in this chapter is organized around the components of natural intelligence identified in previous chapters.

For each gap, the analysis describes what the biological system does, what current AI systems do, where the gap lies, and what research is needed to close it.

The goal is not to be pessimistic about AI progress but to be precise about what remains undone, so that research resources can be directed where they are most needed.

This precision matters because resources are finite, and the history of AI contains many examples of effort expended on approaches that led to dead ends because the fundamental gaps were not clearly identified.

It is worth stating plainly that these ten gaps are not independent items on a checklist; they are deeply interwoven, so that progress on embodiment supports grounding, progress on neuromodulation supports continual learning, and a genuine solution is likely to address several gaps at once rather than each in isolation.

A fair analysis must also confront an obvious objection, that the reasoning models and agents described in the previous chapter have already begun to close these gaps.

They have narrowed some of them, and this book takes that progress seriously.

A model that reasons step by step for a full minute before answering does better at multi-step problems, and an agent that can run code and check its own work catches mistakes that a single-shot chatbot would confidently repeat.

Yet the gaps identified here have proven stubborn precisely because they survive these improvements.

A reasoning model still learns nothing permanent from the problem it just solved, because its weights are frozen after training, so the sample-efficiency gap and the continual-learning gap remain.

The same model that scores well on a familiar mathematics competition still collapses on ARC-AGI-2 puzzles that a child would find easy, which is the signature of a system that pattern-matches within its training distribution rather than reasoning about genuinely novel structure.

Reasoning and tool use, in other words, are powerful additions layered on top of the existing paradigm, not solutions to the deeper architectural differences catalogued below.

Gap One: Sample Efficiency:

Biological systems learn from very few examples.

A child acquires a vocabulary of tens of thousands of words through informal, unsupervised exposure to natural speech and conversation.

A human who has never played chess before can learn to play a recognizable game after watching one or two games.

An octopus can solve a novel puzzle on the first attempt by observing another octopus solving it.

Current AI systems require orders of magnitude more data to achieve comparable performance.

Large language models are trained on trillions of tokens of text.

Image classifiers require millions of labeled examples.

Reinforcement learning systems require millions of interactions.

The gap in sample efficiency between biological and artificial learning is one of the most fundamental differences between NI and AI, and it is not merely a matter of degree, because the biological learner is bringing a lifetime of prior structure to each new problem rather than starting from a blank slate.

Closing this gap requires understanding the mechanisms that make biological learning so data-efficient: hierarchical feature reuse, compositional generalization, strong inductive biases derived from evolutionary preparation, active learning through exploration, and the integration of new knowledge with rich prior knowledge bases.

Research priorities include Meta-learning (learning to learn - the development of AI systems that, through experience with many different tasks, acquire inductive biases that allow them to learn new tasks quickly from very few examples), one-shot and few-shot learning architectures, better integration of structured prior knowledge with statistical learning, and embodied learning through active exploration.

Gap Two: Catastrophic Forgetting and Continual Learning:

Biological organisms learn continuously throughout life without erasing previously acquired knowledge.

A person who learns to play the piano does not forget how to ride a bicycle.

A doctor who learns a new medical procedure does not forget the procedures they already knew.

AI systems currently suffer from catastrophic forgetting (the tendency of an artificial neural network to completely erase previously learned knowledge when trained on a new task).

When an AI system learns task B, the global gradient updates alter the parameters optimized for task A, rendering the system incapable of performing the original task without complete retraining from scratch.

This is the engineering face of the stability-plasticity dilemma, the requirement that a learning system be plastic enough to absorb the new yet stable enough to retain the old, a balance biology strikes routinely and artificial networks strike only crudely.

Biological organisms avoid catastrophic forgetting through the complementary learning systems architecture, sleep-based memory consolidation, neuromodulatory gating of plasticity, and structural mechanisms that protect stable knowledge from overwriting.

Research priorities include implementing a genuine two-system architecture with fast episodic and slow semantic components, offline consolidation processes analogous to sleep-based memory replay, mechanisms for selectively protecting important knowledge while allowing flexible updating of less important knowledge, and integration of episodic and semantic memory systems.

Gap Three: Causal Reasoning and World Modeling:

Biological intelligence reasons about causes and effects, can mentally simulate counterfactuals, and can plan multi-step action sequences by predicting their outcomes.

A child understands that dropping a glass will cause it to break before it hits the floor.

A hunter-gatherer can plan a multi-day route through territory based on a mental model of the landscape.

A scientist can reason about what experiment would distinguish between competing hypotheses.

Current AI systems are trained on statistical correlations and systematically struggle with genuine causal reasoning.

They can identify patterns but fail to reliably distinguish cause from effect or to reason about what would happen if circumstances were changed, the difference, in Judea Pearl's terms, between merely seeing and actually doing.

Research priorities include algorithms that can infer causal structure from observational data, AI systems that learn disentangled representations in which independent causal factors are represented separately, integration of causal models with neural learning systems in hybrid architectures, and incorporation of physical intuitions about causality into AI world models.

Gap Four: Embodiment and Grounded Understanding:

Natural intelligence emerged through billions of years of organisms interacting with physical environments.

The body shapes cognition in fundamental ways.

Motor interaction teaches physics.

Sensory interaction teaches causality.

Environmental interaction teaches adaptation.

Current AI systems are largely disembodied - they process data without existing in or interacting with a physical world.

This creates the Symbol Grounding Problem described in Chapter 8.

Disembodied systems may lack important grounding mechanisms that are necessary for genuine understanding rather than sophisticated pattern matching.

Research priorities include robotic learning systems that can learn general-purpose manipulation and navigation skills from minimal supervision, simulation-to-real transfer methods that produce skills transferring effectively to real physical systems, language grounding that connects language representations to perceptual and sensorimotor experience, multi-modal learning systems integrating visual, auditory, proprioceptive, and tactile information in a unified representation, and developmental approaches that build competence progressively as biological organisms do.

Gap Five: The Architectural Gap - Von Neumann Versus Biological Computing:

Silicon systems separate execution logic from data storage, creating systemic latency and vulnerability.

Biological brains merge execution and storage, ensuring that localized damage causes graceful degradation rather than total system failure.

Artificial networks rely on continuous, synchronized floating-point arithmetic across synchronized layers.

Natural networks utilize asynchronous, event-driven temporal spikes that leverage time itself as a core computational variable.

The human brain operates on approximately twenty watts.

Large AI training runs consume megawatts.

Research priorities include physically implemented neuromorphic computing hardware that executes spiking neural networks natively, temporal coding mechanisms where information is represented by precise arrival times of electrical pulses, and three-dimensional silicon integration stacking memristive layers vertically to replicate the density of biological neural circuits.

A memristor is an electrical component that regulates the flow of electrical current in a circuit while simultaneously remembering the amount of charge that previously passed through it, physically instantiating the co-localization of memory and processing that biology achieves through synaptic structure.

Gap Six: Neuromodulation and Context-Sensitive Learning:

Biological intelligence uses chemical neuromodulators to dynamically adjust the learning rate, attention, and plasticity of neural circuits based on global states including reward, novelty, arousal, and threat level.

A single dopamine signal can cause circuits throughout the brain to simultaneously update their connection strengths in a reward-predictive direction.

A surge of norepinephrine during a threatening situation causes wide-scale changes in what the brain attends to and remembers.

Current AI systems have no equivalent mechanism.

They operate with static, unyielding mathematical hyperparameters (adjustable settings that govern training behavior) that do not dynamically adapt to environmental context, so that a single global learning rate, fixed in advance, plays the role that a rich chemistry of context-sensitive signals plays in the brain.

Research priorities include developing generalized STDP equations that incorporate synthetic neuromodulators, building simulated chemical diffusion fields within software networks that allow virtual nodes to release digital variants of dopamine, acetylcholine, and serotonin, and implementing meta-plasticity algorithms that track the structural history of every individual parameter to prevent runaway saturation.

Gap Seven: Energy Efficiency:

The human brain operates on approximately 20 watts of power - comparable to a dim lightbulb.

It achieves this remarkable efficiency through sparse coding, precise spike-timing communication, highly efficient synaptic mechanisms, and architectural organization that minimizes long-range communication.

Training a large language model can consume megawatt-hours of electricity.

Running inference on large models consumes significant power per query.

The energy efficiency gap between biological and artificial intelligence is many orders of magnitude.

This matters not only for environmental and economic reasons but because energy efficiency constraints may be a significant factor shaping the architectural organization of biological intelligence, which suggests that copying the brain's efficiency tricks may also mean copying some of the cleverness those tricks forced evolution to discover.

Research priorities include sparse and event-driven computing architectures, neuromorphic hardware that executes computation natively within physical material transitions, algorithms that implement sparse coding inspired by the brain's approach to minimizing energy use, and architectural designs that minimize long-range communication costs.

Gap Eight: Social Intelligence and Theory of Mind:

Human intelligence is distinctively social.

A large portion of the human brain is dedicated to navigating social hierarchies, understanding intentions, and cooperating with others.

Theory of Mind (the ability to attribute mental states including beliefs, intents, desires, emotions, and knowledge to oneself and to others, and to understand that others have minds distinct from one's own) is fundamental to human social functioning.

Theory of Mind enables deception detection, cooperative planning, teaching, empathy, and the interpretation of ambiguous behavior.

Current AI systems lack genuine Theory of Mind.

An LLM can predict what a person might say, but it does not maintain a model of the person as an entity with a distinct internal life, current beliefs, and goals that differ from its own.

This leads to failures in social reasoning, misinterpretation of indirect communication, and inability to model how a human will react to information the AI provides.

Research priorities include multi-agent learning environments that require genuine modeling of other agents' beliefs and intentions, embodied social learning systems, architectures that explicitly represent the beliefs and knowledge states of multiple agents, and computational models of empathy and social emotion.

Gap Nine: Metacognition and Self-Knowledge:

Biological intelligence knows what it knows and what it doesn't know.

It monitors the quality of its own cognitive processes, selects appropriate strategies, and detects its own errors before external feedback.

A skilled human expert knows the limits of their expertise and knows when to defer to others.

AI systems currently lack reliable metacognition.

Large language models are notorious for generating confident-sounding outputs even when they are factually incorrect - a failure of confidence monitoring that poses serious risks when these systems are deployed in medical, legal, scientific, or other high-stakes applications.

This failure is closely tied to the phenomenon of hallucination, in which a model produces fluent, plausible, and wholly fabricated information, precisely because it has no internal sense of the boundary between what it knows and what it is merely generating.

Building AI systems with genuine metacognitive ability is one of the most important practical research priorities, both for capability and for safety.

Gap Ten: Consciousness and Deep Integration:

The brain integrates information from perception, memory, emotion, motivation, and bodily state into a unified experience of the present moment.

This deep integration may be what allows biological intelligence to respond coherently to complex situations that require simultaneously considering many interacting factors.

Current AI systems process information in largely modular ways without this deep integration.

Whether consciousness is necessary for AGI remains controversial, but the integration that consciousness may represent is likely necessary for the kind of flexible, contextually appropriate behavior that defines general intelligence.

Research priorities include computational consciousness modeling, recursive self-model architectures, integrated information measurement, artificial introspection systems, and temporal continuity frameworks that give AI systems persistent internal states across time.

PART THREE: THE RESEARCH AGENDA

CHAPTER 13: RESEARCH PATHWAYS TO BRIDGE THE GAP: THE TECHNICAL AGENDA:

Overview of the Research Agenda:

The gap analysis of Chapter 12 identifies ten major dimensions along which Artificial Intelligence (AI) falls short of Natural Intelligence (NI).

Each of those ten gaps is a place where biological brains routinely do something that today's machines cannot do reliably, cheaply, or at all.

Closing these gaps is not a matter of one clever invention.

It requires a coordinated, multi-decade research agenda spanning neuroscience, cognitive science, computer science, philosophy, and engineering.

This chapter describes the most important research pathways, organized by their relationship to the identified gaps.

It is not intended as an exhaustive catalog of all relevant research.

It is intended as a strategic map of the highest-leverage research directions - the research that is most likely to unlock genuine progress toward Artificial General Intelligence (AGI).

A useful way to read this chapter is to treat each pathway as a hypothesis about which missing biological principle matters most.

If a pathway is correct, then importing its principle into machines should produce a capability that scaling alone has failed to deliver.

The pathways are presented separately for clarity, but in a mature AGI program they would be pursued together, because the brain does not implement them in isolation either.

Research Pathway One: Predictive Processing and Active Inference:

The predictive processing framework may be one of the most powerful theoretical frameworks available for guiding the design of brain-inspired AI systems. [6] [7]

The core idea is that intelligence arises from systems that continuously predict their sensory inputs and update their predictions based on prediction errors, rather than passively processing whatever inputs arrive.

In this view the brain is not a stimulus-response machine but a prediction machine that is always running a model of the world slightly ahead of the world itself.

The foundational technical statement of this idea in neuroscience is the predictive coding model of the visual cortex published by Rajesh Rao and Dana Ballard in 1999.

Rao and Ballard proposed that higher levels of the cortex continuously send predictions downward to lower levels, and lower levels send back only the prediction error - the part of the signal that was not anticipated.

This arrangement is extremely efficient, because a perfectly predicted input generates almost no upward traffic, and the system spends its limited bandwidth only on what is surprising.

A familiar everyday example is that you stop consciously hearing a steady air conditioner within minutes, yet you notice instantly when it switches off, because the silence violates a standing prediction.

Karl Friston's Free Energy Principle provides a mathematical formalization of this idea that unifies perception, learning, action, and attention in a single coherent framework.

Free energy here is a quantity borrowed from statistical physics that serves as a tractable upper bound on surprise, defined as the gap between what a system predicts and what it actually senses.

On this account, biological systems act to minimize free energy - a quantity closely related to the difference between predictions and observations - either by updating their internal model of the world or by taking actions that bring the world into correspondence with their predictions.

The second of those options is called active inference, the extension of predictive processing to action, in which actions are understood as a way of fulfilling predictions about one's own sensory inputs.

Under active inference, when you reach for a cup you are in effect predicting the sensation of holding it, and your motor system moves your arm to make that prediction come true.

This dissolves the artificial boundary between perception and control that most current AI systems still maintain, because perceiving and acting become two ways of reducing the same prediction error.

A practical reason this framework matters for engineering is that recent work has shown predictive coding can closely approximate backpropagation, the standard training algorithm of deep learning, while using only local computations.

Researchers including Beren Millidge, James Whittington, and Rafal Bogacz have demonstrated that a predictive coding network, in which each unit updates using only signals from its immediate neighbors, can match the results of backpropagation on deep architectures.

This is significant because backpropagation requires a biologically implausible global pass of error signals through the entire network, whereas predictive coding is local, parallel, and far closer to how real neurons could learn.

Implementing the predictive processing framework in AI systems is therefore a major research direction with both scientific and hardware payoffs.

Research priorities in this area include developing scalable, computationally tractable implementations of predictive coding for large-scale AI systems, extending predictive processing models to include active inference, implementing neuromodulatory regulation within the predictive processing framework, and connecting predictive processing with reinforcement learning in a unified framework.

Reinforcement learning is the branch of machine learning in which an agent learns by receiving rewards or penalties for its actions, and unifying it with active inference would let a single system treat seeking reward and reducing uncertainty as aspects of one objective.

Research Project Idea: Build a hierarchical predictive coding network trained on multi-modal sensorimotor data from robotic platforms.

◦ Implement active inference so the robot acts to confirm its predictions rather than passively receiving data.

◦ Measure learning efficiency and generalization against conventional deep learning baselines on tasks that require genuine adaptation to novel situations.

◦ Track how much sensory bandwidth the predictive system saves by transmitting only prediction errors, which is a direct test of biological efficiency claims.

Research Pathway Two: Complementary Learning Systems for Lifelong Learning:

As described in Chapter 5, the brain solves the stability-plasticity dilemma through a complementary learning systems architecture.

The stability-plasticity dilemma is the challenge of learning new things rapidly without overwriting what has already been learned, and it is one of the deepest unsolved problems in machine learning.

The Complementary Learning Systems (CLS) theory was articulated by James McClelland, Bruce McNaughton, and Randall O'Reilly in an influential 1995 paper in the journal Psychological Review.

Their proposal is that the brain uses two cooperating memory systems with deliberately different learning speeds.

The hippocampus, a seahorse-shaped structure deep in the brain, is the fast system that rapidly captures individual experiences after a single exposure.

The neocortex, the brain's wrinkled outer sheet, is the slow system that gradually extracts general structure across many experiences without disrupting what it already knows.

During sleep the hippocampus replays recent experiences to the neocortex, allowing the slow system to weave new knowledge into old knowledge a little at a time.

Implementing something analogous in AI is essential for achieving lifelong learning without catastrophic forgetting, where a network trained on a new task abruptly loses its ability to perform an old one.

It is worth noting that this biological idea has already paid off once: the experience replay buffer that made deep reinforcement learning practical, used in DeepMind's 2015 Atari-playing system, was explicitly inspired by hippocampal replay.

Current approaches to the forgetting problem include Elastic Weight Consolidation (EWC) - a technique introduced by James Kirkpatrick and colleagues at DeepMind in 2017 that protects the weights most important for previously learned tasks from being overwritten by penalizing changes to those weights during new training.

A useful analogy for EWC is hardening into place the bricks that hold up a finished wall while leaving the mortar of unused regions soft enough to build something new.

Other current approaches include Progressive Neural Networks, architectures that add new modules for each new task while freezing learned weights from previous tasks, Memory-Augmented Neural Networks, architectures that include explicit external memory modules capable of rapid storage and retrieval, and Experience Replay, techniques that maintain a buffer of past training examples and regularly replay them during new learning to prevent forgetting.

These approaches are promising but not yet satisfactory, because they tend to scale poorly, require knowing task boundaries in advance, or consume ever-growing memory.

A more biologically grounded approach would implement a genuine two-system architecture with a fast hippocampal-like system and a slow neocortical-like system, an offline consolidation process analogous to sleep-based memory replay, mechanisms for selectively protecting important knowledge while allowing flexible updating of less important knowledge, and integration of episodic and semantic memory systems in a single unified architecture.

Episodic memory is memory for specific events tied to a time and place, while semantic memory is memory for general facts stripped of their original context, and a mature system needs both and needs to convert one into the other over time.

Research Project Idea: Develop sleep-like states in AI systems where models replay and consolidate experiences during offline periods.

◦ Measure improvements in generalization and resistance to forgetting compared to systems without consolidation.

◦ Compare performance before and after consolidation interventions across diverse task sequences that span multiple domains.

◦ Investigate whether generative replay, in which the system regenerates plausible past experiences rather than storing them verbatim, can match biological consolidation while saving memory.

Research Pathway Three: Causal Representation Learning:

Building AI systems that learn the causal structure of their environment rather than just statistical correlations is one of the highest-priority research directions for closing the gap between NI and AI.

The distinction matters because correlation tells you what tends to occur together, while causation tells you what will happen if you intervene, and only the latter supports reliable planning.

A classic illustration is that ice cream sales and drowning deaths rise together, but banning ice cream would not save a single swimmer, because the hidden common cause is hot weather.

Current deep learning systems are extraordinary correlation engines, which is exactly why they fail in unfamiliar conditions where the old correlations no longer hold.

The theoretical foundation for this work is provided by Judea Pearl's do-calculus, a mathematical framework for reasoning about interventions and counterfactuals in causal systems, for which Pearl received the 2011 Turing Award.

A counterfactual is a claim about what would have happened under a different action, such as whether a patient would have recovered had they not taken a drug, and answering such questions is something humans do effortlessly and machines do poorly.

The do-calculus works alongside the framework of Structural Causal Models (SCMs) - mathematical models that represent the causal relationships between variables in a system using directed graphs and structural equations.

In a structural causal model, an arrow from one variable to another means that wiggling the first directly changes the second, which is a far stronger and more useful claim than mere statistical association.

A central modern research program, set out in the 2021 paper "Toward Causal Representation Learning" by Bernhard Scholkopf, Yoshua Bengio, and colleagues in the Proceedings of the IEEE, argues that machine learning must learn the causal variables themselves, not just relationships among hand-chosen variables.

Their guiding idea is the principle of independent causal mechanisms, the assumption that the world is built from separate, reusable cause-and-effect modules that can be recombined, which is why a child who learns how shadows work can apply that knowledge to any object.

Research priorities include algorithms that can infer causal structure from observational data, AI systems that learn disentangled representations, integration of causal models with neural learning systems in hybrid architectures, and counterfactual data augmentation.
