A further complication is the rebound effect known as Jevons paradox, in which efficiency gains lower the cost of computation and thereby increase its total consumption rather than reducing it.

Energy spent on inference, meaning the day-to-day running of deployed models, has come to rival or exceed the energy spent on training them, so efficiency at deployment now matters as much as efficiency during learning.

The contrast with biological intelligence is stark and demands scientific explanation.

1.2 What Biology Achieves

The brain achieves its computational power through mechanisms that differ fundamentally from the arithmetic operations performed by digital chips.

Biological neurons, meaning individual nerve cells, operate asynchronously, meaning they fire only when specific threshold conditions are met rather than cycling through operations on a fixed clock.

This event-driven property, sometimes called sparse coding because only a small fraction of neurons are active at any moment, means that the majority of a biological neural network is consuming very little energy at any given instant.

Additional efficiency gains arise from the tight co-location of memory and computation in synapses, meaning the connection points between neurons, which eliminates the energy cost of moving data between separate memory and processing units as conventional digital computers must do.

Biological systems also exploit analog signal processing, meaning they use graded continuous signals rather than discrete binary values, which can represent information with fewer physical operations.

Individual neurons are themselves nontrivial computers, because their branching dendrites can perform local nonlinear operations rather than acting as simple summing points, multiplying the effective processing per cell.

Theoretical work suggests biological synapses operate remarkably close to the fundamental thermodynamic limits on the energy cost of erasing information, a bound formalized by Rolf Landauer.

1.3 The Neuromorphic Research Gap

Neuromorphic computing, the interdisciplinary field that attempts to build hardware inspired by these biological principles, has produced important prototype systems including IBM's TrueNorth chip, Intel's Loihi and Loihi 2 chips, and the BrainScaleS-2 platform developed at Heidelberg University.

The field traces to Carver Mead, who in the late 1980s argued that the physics of transistors operated in their low-power analog regime could directly mimic the physics of neurons rather than merely simulating them with digital arithmetic.

These systems demonstrate that spiking neural network, meaning networks whose artificial neurons fire discrete event pulses rather than passing continuous values, implementations on specialized hardware can achieve energy-efficiency improvements of one to three orders of magnitude over conventional graphics processing units for specific tasks.

However, a critical gap remains: no study has systematically isolated and quantified the independent contribution of each proposed biological efficiency principle in comparable hardware implementations across an equivalent range of cognitive tasks.

Without this isolation, hardware designers cannot determine which principles deserve the greatest engineering investment and which are secondary.

The practical danger of skipping this step is that the field may invest heavily in whichever principle is easiest to publish rather than whichever principle actually carries most of the efficiency, an error that a controlled decomposition is designed to prevent.

This study addresses that gap directly.

2. RESEARCH QUESTIONS

Primary Research Question:

Which specific computational principles of biological neural systems contribute most to energy efficiency, and what is the relative magnitude of each principle's contribution when implemented in comparable hardware platforms?

Secondary Research Questions:

◦ Can the identified principles be implemented in current neuromorphic hardware without loss of task performance relative to conventional digital implementations?

◦ Do the efficiency contributions of individual principles interact multiplicatively or additively when combined?

◦ Does the relative contribution of each principle vary with task type, specifically perceptual tasks versus reasoning tasks versus learning tasks?

◦ What engineering constraints limit the translation of each biological principle into hardware, and can those constraints be quantified?

3. HYPOTHESES

Null Hypothesis (H0):

No individual computational principle derived from biological neural systems produces a statistically significant improvement in energy efficiency per unit of task performance when implemented in neuromorphic hardware compared to a conventional digital baseline.

Alternative Hypothesis H1 (Sparse Coding Dominance):

Sparse coding, meaning limiting simultaneous neural activation to a small fraction of the total network, will be identified as the single largest contributor to energy efficiency, accounting for more than 40 percent of the total efficiency advantage observed in biological systems.

Alternative Hypothesis H2 (Synaptic Co-location):

The co-location of memory and computation in synaptic structures, which eliminates von Neumann bottleneck data movement costs, will produce the largest efficiency gains on memory-intensive tasks such as pattern recognition and associative recall.

Alternative Hypothesis H3 (Multiplicative Interaction):

The combination of multiple biological efficiency principles will produce efficiency gains greater than the sum of the gains from each principle applied individually, indicating multiplicative interaction effects.

4. METHODOLOGY

4.1 Experimental Design

This study employs a factorial benchmarking design with the following factors:

Computational Principle (6 levels): ◦ Sparse asynchronous spiking ◦ In-memory computation at the synapse level ◦ Analog signal representation ◦ Temporal coding, meaning information encoded in spike timing ◦ Structural pruning and weight sparsity ◦ Local learning rules without global backpropagation

Hardware Platform (4 levels): ◦ Conventional GPU baseline ◦ Intel Loihi 2 neuromorphic chip ◦ BrainScaleS-2 analog neuromorphic system ◦ Custom memristor-based testbed

Task Category (3 levels): ◦ Perceptual classification ◦ Associative memory retrieval ◦ Continual online learning

Each combination will be tested with the target principle fully enabled, fully disabled, and partially enabled at 50 percent implementation fidelity, yielding a complete 6 x 4 x 3 x 3 factorial structure.

4.2 Energy Measurement Protocol

Energy consumption will be measured at the hardware level using calibrated power monitors sampling at a minimum of 1,000 times per second.

All measurements will capture total system power including memory access, data movement, and peripheral operations, not processor core power alone.

Task performance will be measured as accuracy on standardized benchmarks appropriate to each task category.

The primary dependent variable will be energy efficiency defined as correct task outputs per joule of energy consumed, allowing direct cross-platform comparison.

4.3 Benchmark Tasks

Perceptual classification tasks will use the MNIST handwritten digit dataset and the CIFAR-10 image classification dataset as standard computer-vision benchmarks.

Associative memory retrieval tasks will use pattern completion benchmarks derived from Hopfield network test sets.

Continual learning tasks will use the Split-CIFAR-100 protocol, in which a classifier must learn new categories sequentially without forgetting previously learned ones.

4.4 Biological Reference Measurements

To establish the biological baseline against which hardware implementations are compared, metabolic energy consumption data from established neuroimaging studies using positron emission tomography (PET) and functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) will be compiled.

Electrophysiology recordings from publicly available datasets documenting single-unit neural activity in animal visual cortex during classification tasks will be used to estimate biological spike rates and sparsity levels.

4.5 Sample Size and Replication

Each factorial cell will be measured across a minimum of 1,000 independent task trials.

Each hardware measurement will be replicated 10 times with full system reboot between replications to control for thermal and state-dependent variability.

4.6 Statistical Analysis

Mixed-effects analysis of variance will be used to estimate the main effect of each computational principle, the main effects of hardware platform and task category, and all two-way and three-way interaction terms.

Effect sizes will be reported as partial eta-squared.

Significance threshold will be set at p less than 0.01 with Bonferroni correction for multiple comparisons.

Post-hoc pairwise comparisons will use Tukey's Honest Significant Difference test.

5. CONTROLS AND CONFOUNDS

All hardware platforms will execute identical task code at the functional level to ensure that performance differences are attributable to hardware architecture rather than software implementation differences.

Temperature will be controlled within plus or minus 2 degrees Celsius across all measurement sessions to prevent thermal variation from confounding power measurements.

Network architecture size will be matched across platforms in terms of total parameter count to prevent scale effects from masking efficiency differences.

All power measurements will be validated against manufacturer-published power consumption profiles before data collection begins.

6. LIMITATIONS

This study measures hardware-implemented approximations of biological principles rather than biological systems themselves.

The degree to which a given hardware implementation faithfully captures the biological principle it models is itself a source of uncertainty that will be quantified but cannot be fully eliminated.

The study is limited to the six principles listed above; additional biological efficiency mechanisms such as glial cell support functions and neuromodulatory gain control are deferred to future work.

7. SIGNIFICANCE AND EXPECTED CONTRIBUTIONS

This study will produce the first systematic, quantitative decomposition of the energy efficiency advantage of biological neural computation into independently measurable component principles.

Beyond hardware design, the findings would give energy planners and policymakers a principled basis for forecasting the load that AI will place on electrical grids, turning a fast-moving worry into a quantitative model.

They would also discipline the field's habit of importing one biological idea at a time, by showing which principles actually carry the efficiency and which are secondary.

If multiplicative interaction effects are confirmed, the results will provide a principled engineering argument for implementing multiple biological principles simultaneously rather than selecting a single most-efficient principle.

This would mirror biology, in which sparsity, in-memory computation, and analog signaling are not separate tricks but aspects of a single integrated design, and it would argue against the common practice of importing one biological idea at a time.

The results will directly inform hardware design priorities for next-generation neuromorphic computing platforms and will contribute to reducing the unsustainable energy footprint of large-scale AI systems.

Because the energy footprint of AI has become a matter of public policy and grid planning, a principled decomposition of biological efficiency also gives policymakers and energy economists a sounder technical basis for forecasting and regulation.

8. REFERENCES

Davies, M., et al. Advancing neuromorphic computing with Loihi: A survey of results and outlook. Proceedings of the IEEE, 109, 911-934, 2021.

Modha, D.S., et al. Neural inference at the frontier of energy, space, and time. Science, 382, 329-335, 2023.

Stanojevic, A., et al. High-performance deep spiking neural networks with 0.3 spikes per neuron. Nature Communications, 15, 6793, 2024.

Pehle, C., et al. The BrainScaleS-2 accelerated neuromorphic system with hybrid plasticity. Frontiers in Neuroscience, 16, 795876, 2022.

Patterson, D., et al. Carbon emissions and large neural network training. arXiv preprint arXiv:2104.10350, 2021.

Kudithipudi, D., et al. Biological underpinnings for lifelong learning machines. Nature Machine Intelligence, 4, 196-210, 2022.

CHAPTER 27: HOW DOES CONSCIOUSNESS EMERGE FROM NEURAL ACTIVITY, AND IS CONSCIOUSNESS NECESSARY FOR AGI?

Proposed Scientific Experiment

Submitted for Peer Review Consideration

ABSTRACT

Consciousness, defined here as the capacity for subjective experience or what philosophers call phenomenal awareness, meaning the felt quality of seeing red, experiencing pain, or recognizing oneself as a continuous entity, remains one of the most contested and least understood phenomena in science.

Despite more than a century of neuroscientific research and decades of rigorous theoretical development, no consensus exists on which neural mechanisms are sufficient to produce conscious experience, how those mechanisms could be identified in a non-biological system, or whether consciousness is a precondition for the general intelligence that researchers envision in Artificial General Intelligence (AGI), defined as a system capable of performing any cognitive task a human can perform.

The question has lately moved from the seminar room into the laboratory, with rival theories now issuing preregistered, falsifiable predictions rather than trading intuitions.

The 2023 adversarial collaboration in which proponents of Integrated Information Theory and Global Workspace Theory agreed in advance on the experiments that would count against each is a model of how a notoriously slippery question can be made to yield to evidence.

This paper proposes a multi-stage experimental program designed to test competing theoretical accounts of consciousness against empirical neural data, to develop candidate markers that could in principle be applied to artificial systems, and to empirically evaluate whether the cognitive functions most associated with consciousness in biological systems are in fact necessary for general-purpose intelligent performance.

Its strength is that it pits the major theories against one another on the same data rather than testing each alone, so that where their predicted neural signatures agree or diverge becomes itself a finding.

Extending the same analysis to artificial systems would, for the first time, test whether consciousness-linked architectural features confer any measurable cognitive advantage.

1. INTRODUCTION AND MOTIVATION

1.1 The Hard Problem and the Scientific Problem

The philosopher David Chalmers distinguished between what he called the easy problems of consciousness, meaning explaining specific cognitive functions such as attention, reportability, and behavioral integration, and the hard problem, meaning explaining why any physical process gives rise to subjective experience at all.

The hard problem remains philosophically unresolved.

The philosopher Thomas Nagel framed the core difficulty in his 1974 essay by asking what it is like to be a bat, pointing out that an organism is conscious if there is something it is like to be that organism, a fact that may be real yet inaccessible from the outside.

However, a scientific research program does not require solving the hard problem in order to make progress on the questions that matter most for AGI design: which neural or computational processes reliably correlate with conscious states, and which cognitive capabilities depend on those processes.

1.2 Leading Theoretical Frameworks

Integrated Information Theory (IIT), proposed by neuroscientist Giulio Tononi, holds that consciousness is identical to a specific kind of integrated information, measured by a quantity called phi, and that any physical system exceeding a threshold phi value possesses some degree of consciousness.

Global Neuronal Workspace Theory (GNW), associated with Bernard Baars and Jean-Pierre Changeux, holds that consciousness arises when information is broadcast globally across a distributed workspace of cortical areas, making it available to multiple downstream cognitive processes simultaneously.

Higher-Order Theories hold that a mental state is conscious only when it is represented by a higher-order mental state, meaning a state that is about the first state.

Predictive Processing frameworks hold that consciousness arises from the brain's ongoing generative modeling of sensory input and its own internal states.

Each theory makes distinct empirical predictions about which neural signatures should accompany conscious versus non-conscious processing.

Importantly, these theories are now being tested against one another directly, as in the large adversarial collaboration reported in 2023 that pitted Integrated Information Theory against Global Neuronal Workspace Theory using preregistered predictions, a model of how contested science can be moved forward.

1.3 Why the AGI Connection Matters

A 2024 survey of 582 AI researchers found that 25 percent expected AI consciousness within ten years and 60 percent expected it eventually.

Whether or not AI systems can or should be conscious is one of the most consequential design questions in the field.

The danger runs in two directions at once, since systems that merely imitate the outward signs of feeling may invite people to over-attribute experience to them, while genuinely sentient systems, if they ever arise, might be wrongly dismissed as mere machines.

If consciousness is architecturally necessary for general reasoning, flexible planning, or genuine self-modeling, then AGI designs that ignore it may hit fundamental performance ceilings.

If consciousness is not necessary for those functions, then it becomes a welfare and ethics question rather than an engineering one.

Either way the question is no longer purely theoretical, because as systems grow more sophisticated the cost of wrongly assuming they cannot suffer, or wrongly assuming they can, both become serious.

This study addresses the empirical question directly.

2. RESEARCH QUESTIONS

Primary Research Question:

Which neural signatures most reliably distinguish conscious from non-conscious processing in biological systems, and do those signatures predict performance on tasks requiring general cognitive flexibility?

Secondary Research Questions:

◦ Do the signatures predicted by IIT, GNW, Higher-Order Theory, and Predictive Processing frameworks converge on the same neural events, or do they dissociate?

◦ Can any currently measurable property of an artificial neural network serve as a valid proxy for the neural signatures identified in biological systems?

◦ Are there cognitive task categories for which conscious processing is necessary for above-chance performance in biological systems?

◦ Does the presence or absence of consciousness-correlated signatures in artificial systems predict their performance on general cognitive flexibility benchmarks?

3. HYPOTHESES

Null Hypothesis (H0):

Neural signatures associated with conscious processing in biological systems do not predict cognitive flexibility performance above the level predicted by task-specific neural activity alone.

Alternative Hypothesis H1 (GNW Prediction):

Global broadcast signatures, meaning the widespread synchronous activation of prefrontal and parietal cortical areas observed in GNW theory, will be the strongest predictors of flexible cognitive performance across task domains, outperforming localized neural signatures.

Alternative Hypothesis H2 (IIT Prediction):

The integrated information measure phi, computed from neural connectivity patterns during task performance, will positively correlate with the breadth of cognitive flexibility across domains.

Alternative Hypothesis H3 (Functional Sufficiency):

Artificial systems that implement the functional architecture associated with conscious processing in biological systems, specifically global information broadcast and hierarchical self-modeling, will outperform systems lacking these features on novel generalization benchmarks even without any phenomenal awareness.

4. METHODOLOGY

4.1 Biological Study: Human Neuroimaging

Participants will be healthy adult volunteers recruited with full informed consent under an approved ethics protocol.

A minimum of 60 participants will be recruited, providing statistical power greater than 0.90 for the primary analysis at a medium effect size.

Participants will perform a battery of cognitive tasks under conditions designed to vary the degree of conscious access to task-relevant information.

Masking paradigms, meaning presentations of stimuli too brief or obscured for conscious recognition, will be used to create matched conscious and non-conscious processing conditions for the same stimuli.

Simultaneous 256-channel electroencephalography (EEG) and functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) will record neural activity throughout all task conditions.

4.2 Neural Signature Extraction

For each theoretical framework, the specific neural signature predicted to mark conscious processing will be extracted from the neuroimaging data.

For GNW: late-latency widespread cortical synchrony (the late global ignition signature).

For IIT: phi-approximation measures computed from functional connectivity matrices.

For Higher-Order Theories: activation in prefrontal areas associated with metacognitive monitoring.

For Predictive Processing: suppression of prediction error signals in sensory cortex following conscious recognition.

4.3 Artificial System Study

Six large neural network architectures representing distinct design philosophies will be selected for comparison.

Each architecture will be evaluated on a battery of cognitive flexibility tasks matched to the human task battery.

Architectural features hypothesized to correspond to biological consciousness signatures will be identified in each system and their presence scored on a structured rubric.

The relationship between these architectural features and task performance will be assessed using the same analytical framework applied to biological data.

4.4 Dependent Measures

Sensitivity and specificity of each neural signature for classifying conscious versus non-conscious trials in the human data.

Correlation between neural signature strength and cognitive flexibility performance across task domains.

Cross-architecture correlation between consciousness-proxy architectural features and general-task performance in artificial systems.

4.5 Statistical Analysis

Within-participant repeated-measures analyses will test the reliability of neural signatures across task domains.

Cross-validation will ensure that signature-performance correlations generalize to held-out tasks.

Bayesian model comparison will assess the relative evidential support for each theoretical framework's predictions.

5. CONTROLS AND CONFOUNDS

Task difficulty will be matched across conscious and non-conscious conditions using pre-tested stimulus parameters.

Participant vigilance will be monitored continuously using pupillometry and response time variability to prevent drowsiness from confounding conscious versus non-conscious comparisons.

Artificial system evaluations will use task prompts designed to prevent retrieval of memorized answers, ensuring that performance reflects genuine generalization.

6. LIMITATIONS

The study cannot resolve the philosophical hard problem of consciousness.

The artificial system component of the study relies on behavioral and architectural proxies rather than direct access to any subjective experience the systems may or may not have.

This is the same epistemic wall that makes the problem hard in other minds generally, and the study is designed to make progress on measurable correlates rather than to pretend the wall can be removed.

Whether the functional signatures identified in this study are genuinely identical to consciousness or merely reliably correlated with it is a question this experimental design cannot answer.

7. SIGNIFICANCE AND EXPECTED CONTRIBUTIONS

This study will produce the largest comparative empirical evaluation to date of competing neural theories of consciousness against behavioral outcomes relevant to general cognitive flexibility.

If reliable markers of conscious processing can be defined, they would also anchor the emerging discipline of AI welfare assessment, giving developers a principled, if provisional, way to ask whether a system they have built might warrant moral caution.

It will provide the first direct test of whether consciousness-correlated architectural features in artificial systems predict general performance advantages.

The results will inform AGI design choices at a foundational level and will contribute to the emerging scientific field of AI welfare assessment.

8. REFERENCES

Tononi, G., et al. Integrated information theory: from consciousness to its physical substrate. Nature Reviews Neuroscience, 17, 450-461, 2016.

Dehaene, S., Changeux, J.P., and Mashour, G.A. Experimental and theoretical approaches to conscious processing. Neuron, 70, 200-227, 2011.

Schwitzgebel, E. AI and Consciousness. Manuscript, University of California Riverside, 2025.

Identifying indicators of consciousness in AI systems. Trends in Cognitive Sciences, published online December 1, 2024.

Lau, H., and Rosenthal, D. Empirical support for higher-order theories of conscious awareness. Trends in Cognitive Sciences, 15, 365-373, 2011.

Koch, C., Massimini, M., Boly, M., and Tononi, G. Neural correlates of consciousness: progress and problems. Nature Reviews Neuroscience, 17, 307-321, 2016.

CHAPTER 28: CAN AI SYSTEMS DEVELOP GENUINE WORLD MODELS, AND WHAT ARCHITECTURAL FEATURES ARE REQUIRED?

Proposed Scientific Experiment

Submitted for Peer Review Consideration

ABSTRACT

A world model, as used in artificial intelligence research, is an internal representation that an agent constructs to simulate how its environment behaves, predict the consequences of actions before taking them, and plan sequences of behavior without requiring constant physical trial and error.

World models are considered a candidate cornerstone capability for Artificial General Intelligence (AGI), because biological intelligence relies heavily on internal simulation: humans can mentally rehearse actions, anticipate consequences, and reason about counterfactuals, meaning situations that did not happen but could have, without direct environmental interaction.

An agent that carries an accurate internal model can rehearse actions in imagination, testing plans at no physical cost and learning from far fewer real trials, which is the difference between a system that must touch every stove to learn it is hot and one that can reason that it will be.

The idea has deep roots, from Kenneth Craik's 1943 proposal that the mind builds small-scale models of reality to Edward Tolman's cognitive maps in rats, and its modern form drives systems that learn by imagining future scenarios rather than only by acting.

The appeal is that a system with an accurate internal model can plan by simulation, testing actions in imagination at no physical cost, which is far safer and cheaper than learning everything by trial and error in the world itself.

Current AI systems, including the largest language models and the most capable vision systems, consistently fail on benchmarks requiring world-model competence, showing near-random accuracy on tasks involving causal prediction, physics-consistent reasoning about object trajectories, and robust generalization to novel physical contexts.

This paper proposes a systematic experimental program to determine what architectural features distinguish systems that form genuine internal world models from systems that exploit surface statistical patterns, and to evaluate whether any currently available architecture crosses the threshold into genuine world-model competence.

By certifying every benchmark item as unsolvable by surface pattern matching before any model sees it, the design guards against the contamination that has quietly inflated past world-model claims.

Pairing behavioral tests with internal probing would further reveal whether a system that answers correctly does so by simulating the world or by shortcut.

1. INTRODUCTION AND MOTIVATION

1.1 The World Model Hypothesis

The idea that intelligent agents build and consult internal models of their environments dates to Kenneth Craik's 1943 monograph The Nature of Explanation and was introduced into machine learning by Jurgen Schmidhuber in 1990.

The psychologist Edward Tolman had already shown in 1948 that rats navigate using internal cognitive maps rather than fixed stimulus-response habits, early behavioral evidence that brains build models rather than mere reflexes.

Yann LeCun's 2022 position paper revived and extended this framework, arguing that the absence of a learned world model is the central limitation preventing current AI systems from achieving the kind of general reasoning and robust planning that characterizes human intelligence.

LeCun's proposed remedy, the joint embedding predictive architecture, trains a system to predict in an abstract representation space rather than predicting raw pixels, on the argument that modeling the world at the right level of abstraction is what current systems lack.

Systems including DreamerV3, an agent reported in a 2025 Nature paper, demonstrate that AI agents can improve their behavior by internally imagining future scenarios rather than requiring direct environmental interaction.

Yet a persistent question divides the research community: do such systems genuinely represent and simulate causal environmental dynamics, or do they exploit learned statistical regularities that function like world-model predictions within training distribution but fail outside it?

1.2 The Grounding Problem

Language-only systems lack what researchers call grounding, meaning a connection between internal representations and real-world physical events or objects.

A benchmark paper presented at a 2025 conference reported striking limitations in vision-language models' basic world-modeling abilities, including near-random accuracy when asked to distinguish physically plausible from implausible motion trajectories.

This finding suggests that statistical pattern matching over large datasets, even multimodal datasets combining images and text, does not automatically produce the kind of causal physical model that biological intelligence uses.

The picture is not entirely one-sided, however, because controlled studies of a model trained only on game transcripts found that it had nonetheless built an internal representation of the game board, hinting that genuine world models can sometimes emerge from prediction alone.

1.3 What Genuine World Models Require

Current research suggests that genuine world models require at minimum: causal structure learning, meaning the ability to infer cause-and-effect relationships rather than correlations alone; object persistence, meaning the representation of objects as entities with continuous existence even when not currently observable; and compositional generalization, meaning the ability to apply known rules to novel combinations of known elements.

2. RESEARCH QUESTIONS

Primary Research Question:

Do any currently available AI architectures form internal world models that satisfy operational criteria for causal correctness, object persistence, and compositional generalization, and if so, what architectural features distinguish them from architectures that fail these criteria?

Secondary Research Questions:

◦ Is multimodal grounding, meaning training on both sensory and linguistic data simultaneously, necessary for world-model formation, or is it sufficient to train on one modality alone?

◦ Does the use of explicit structured representations, such as scene graphs or causal graphs, improve world-model quality beyond what is achievable with purely distributed neural representations?

◦ Does an agent with a verified world model show superior sample efficiency, meaning it learns new tasks from fewer examples, compared to agents without verified world models?

◦ Can world-model quality be reliably assessed using behavioral benchmarks alone, or are interpretability tools that examine internal representations necessary?

3. HYPOTHESES

Null Hypothesis (H0):

No currently available AI architecture demonstrates world-model performance that exceeds a statistically equivalent alternative explanation based on sophisticated statistical pattern matching on the benchmark tasks designed for this study.

Alternative Hypothesis H1 (Architecture Matters):

Architectures that include explicit causal structure learning components will demonstrate significantly higher world-model benchmark performance than architectures of equivalent parameter count that use only distributed representations.

Alternative Hypothesis H2 (Embodiment Matters):

Architectures trained with embodied interaction in simulated physical environments will develop higher-fidelity world models than architectures trained on equivalent quantities of observational data without interaction.

Alternative Hypothesis H3 (Compositionality Marker):

Compositional generalization performance, meaning accuracy on tasks combining familiar elements in novel ways, will be the single most discriminative test for genuine world-model possession, outperforming object permanence tasks and causal prediction tasks as a diagnostic.

4. METHODOLOGY

4.1 Benchmark Battery Development

A novel benchmark battery will be developed specifically for this study, designed to be resistant to solution by statistical pattern matching alone.

The battery will include four benchmark classes:

Causal intervention tasks: the agent must predict the outcome of a specific physical intervention that was never shown in training data, requiring genuine causal inference.

Object persistence tasks: the agent must track the inferred location of an object that has been hidden from observation for a variable number of time steps.

Counterfactual reasoning tasks: the agent must reason about what would have happened if a specific event had not occurred.

Compositional novel scenario tasks: the agent must predict behavior in a scenario composed of familiar elements arranged in a combination never present in training data.

All benchmark items will be reviewed by an independent panel of cognitive scientists to certify that they cannot be solved by pattern matching on typical training data distributions.

4.2 Architectural Conditions

Eight AI architectures will be selected for evaluation, spanning:

Large transformer-based language models • Multimodal vision-language models • Explicit symbolic world-model architectures • Hybrid neural-symbolic architectures • Architectures trained with interactive embodied experience • Architecture trained with passive observational data only

All architectures will be evaluated on identical benchmark tasks.

4.3 Interpretability Analysis

For each architecture that passes behavioral threshold criteria on the benchmark battery, internal activation analysis will be conducted to determine whether the system contains representations with properties consistent with object tracking, causal structure encoding, and predictive simulation.

Probing classifiers, meaning small secondary networks trained to decode specific properties from an architecture's internal representations, will be used to test whether world-model-relevant information is encoded in internal states even in cases where behavioral performance is poor.

4.4 Dependent Measures

Accuracy on each of the four benchmark classes • Sample efficiency on novel task learning following benchmark evaluation • Internal representation probing accuracy for world-model-relevant properties • Out-of-distribution generalization: performance on tasks drawn from physical domains not included in training

4.5 Analysis

Confirmatory analyses will test the primary hypothesis using pre-registered one-sided superiority tests with alpha set at 0.01.

Exploratory analyses will examine the relationship between specific architectural features and each benchmark class using regression modeling.

5. CONTROLS AND CONFOUNDS

Training data content will be audited to ensure that no benchmark item or item closely similar to any benchmark item appears in any system's training data.

Parameter count will be reported for all systems and included as a covariate in all analyses to prevent scale confounds.

All benchmark evaluations will use zero-shot protocols, meaning systems receive no examples of the benchmark task before evaluation, to prevent in-context learning from mimicking world-model competence.

6. LIMITATIONS

The study evaluates world-model proxies in current-generation AI systems.

Whether any positive results indicate that the system has a world model in the full theoretical sense, including genuine causal representation, versus a sophisticated approximation that happens to generalize well on the benchmark, will require additional interpretability research beyond the scope of this study.

7. SIGNIFICANCE AND EXPECTED CONTRIBUTIONS

This study will provide the first systematically controlled, multi-architecture evaluation of world-model competence in AI systems using benchmarks explicitly designed to resist solution by pattern matching.

A benchmark deliberately built to defeat memorization would also act as a moving target for the field, exposing the gap between systems that have genuinely modeled a domain and those that have merely interpolated within it.

If any architecture demonstrates consistent world-model competence across all four benchmark classes, this will constitute the strongest evidence to date that a specific architectural approach enables genuine causal world modeling.

If all architectures fail, this will establish a clear empirical benchmark gap and motivate specific architectural innovations.

A clean negative result would itself be valuable, because much of the current debate rests on anecdotes about model successes and failures rather than on benchmarks deliberately built to be immune to memorization.

The interpretability component will provide the research community's most detailed examination to date of what world-model-relevant information is or is not encoded in the internal representations of state-of-the-art AI systems.

8. REFERENCES

LeCun, Y. A path towards autonomous machine intelligence. OpenReview, 2022.

Schmidhuber, J. Making the world differentiable: on using self-supervised fully recurrent neural networks for dynamic reinforcement learning and planning in non-stationary environments. Technical Report FKI-126-90, Technical University Munich, 1990.

Hafner, D., et al. Mastering diverse domains through world models. Nature, 2025.

Gupta, T., and Pruthi, D. Beyond world models: rethinking understanding in AI models. arXiv:2511.12239, 2026.

Andreas, J. Language models as agent models. arXiv preprint, 2024.

CHAPTER 29: IS EMBODIMENT NECESSARY FOR AGI, AND IF SO, WHAT DEGREE OF EMBODIMENT IS REQUIRED?

Proposed Scientific Experiment

Submitted for Peer Review Consideration

ABSTRACT

Embodiment, meaning the possession of a physical body that senses and acts within a physical environment, is widely argued in cognitive science and philosophy to be a foundational prerequisite for genuinely general intelligence rather than a merely useful practical feature.

The embodied cognition research tradition, associated with scholars including Francisco Varela, Evan Thompson, and Eleanor Rosch, holds that many cognitive capacities, including spatial reasoning, temporal sequencing, social understanding, and grounded language comprehension, develop only through the sensorimotor interaction of a body with a physical world.

If this view is correct, then AI systems trained exclusively on text or image data, regardless of scale, will necessarily lack critical cognitive capacities that require embodied developmental experience.

The answer carries enormous practical weight, because it determines whether tens of billions of dollars of investment should flow toward robotics and physical interaction or toward ever-larger models trained on static data, a question that has so far been argued largely by anecdote.

Moravec's paradox sharpens the stakes, since the sensorimotor skills that embodiment would supply are exactly the ones that have proven hardest to obtain from data alone.

If the view is incorrect, or if partial degrees of embodiment can substitute for full physical embodiment, then the engineering path to AGI may not require building and deploying physical robotic systems at scale.

This is not merely a philosophical dispute, because the answer determines whether tens of billions of dollars should flow toward robotics and physical interaction or toward ever-larger models trained on static data.

This study proposes a systematic experimental evaluation of the embodiment hypothesis across a range of cognitive task domains and a range of embodiment levels from purely passive textual data to full robotic physical interaction.

Treating embodiment as a graded continuum from text-only to full robotic interaction, rather than as a yes-or-no property, lets the study locate any threshold at which specific competencies first appear.

A matched data-only control at each level is what separates the effect of embodied interaction from the effect of simply seeing more.

1. INTRODUCTION AND MOTIVATION

1.1 The Embodied Cognition Tradition

The claim that intelligence requires a body is not new.

The roboticist Hans Moravec captured the puzzle that motivates this field with the observation now called Moravec's paradox, that abstract reasoning is comparatively easy to mechanize while the sensorimotor skills of a toddler are extraordinarily hard.

John Dewey argued in 1896 that perception and action form an inseparable loop rather than a sequential chain of input and output.

More recently, neuroscientists including Antonio Damasio have argued that the body's physiological states are constitutively involved in emotional reasoning and decision-making, not merely inputs to a disembodied cognitive processor.

In developmental psychology, Piaget's account of sensorimotor stages documents how children first construct concepts of object permanence, spatial relations, and causal agency through physical manipulation before those concepts can be represented abstractly.

The roboticist Rodney Brooks pushed the idea to its limit in 1991 with the slogan that intelligence requires no central representation, building robots whose competence emerged directly from tight loops between sensing and acting.

1.2 The AI Relevance

Current large language models are trained on text without any sensorimotor grounding.

They can describe physical actions in detail, predict sequences of events, and reason about spatial relationships, but evaluations consistently reveal failures on tasks requiring genuine physical intuition, such as novel object manipulation, tool use in unfamiliar contexts, and robust spatial navigation in environments differing from training scenarios.

Embodied AI research, which combines language and vision models with simulated or robotic physical systems, has grown substantially in 2024 and 2025, but the specific cognitive gains attributable to embodiment, as opposed to additional training data of any modality, have not been systematically isolated.

A subtle prediction of the embodied view is morphological computation, the idea that the body itself performs part of the cognitive work, as when the spring-like mechanics of legs simplify the control problem of walking.

1.3 Degrees of Embodiment

Rather than treating embodiment as binary, this study distinguishes five levels along a continuum:

Level 0: Text-only training with no sensory or action data.

Level 1: Passive visual observation of physical scenes without action.

Level 2: Simulated interaction in virtual physical environments.

Level 3: Teleoperated robotic interaction with intermittent feedback.

Level 4: Fully autonomous robotic embodiment with continuous sensorimotor feedback loops.

2. RESEARCH QUESTIONS

Primary Research Question:

Does increasing the degree of embodiment, across the five levels defined above, produce measurable improvements in cognitive task performance that cannot be explained by equivalent increases in training data quantity alone?

Secondary Research Questions:

◦ For which specific cognitive task domains is full physical embodiment necessary, and for which is simulated embodiment sufficient?

◦ Is there a minimum degree of embodiment below which specific cognitive capacities fail to develop regardless of training scale?

◦ Do embodiment-derived cognitive gains transfer to tasks in domains entirely different from the embodiment training context?

◦ What is the relationship between the temporal duration of embodied experience and the cognitive gains produced?

3. HYPOTHESES

Null Hypothesis (H0):

Increasing embodiment level produces no cognitive task performance gains beyond those produced by equivalent quantities of additional training data in any modality, when task-relevant training data content is matched.

Alternative Hypothesis H1 (Graded Embodiment):

Each step up the embodiment continuum produces statistically significant and independent cognitive performance gains over the level below it, consistent with a graded rather than threshold model of embodiment benefits.

Alternative Hypothesis H2 (Threshold Embodiment):

A critical threshold exists, predicted to be at Level 2 or above, below which certain cognitive capacities do not develop, and above which they emerge reliably regardless of additional training scale.

Alternative Hypothesis H3 (Domain Specificity):

Embodiment advantages will be domain-specific: they will be strong for spatial reasoning, object manipulation, and causal physical prediction, but negligible for language comprehension, formal reasoning, and mathematical problem-solving.

4. METHODOLOGY

4.1 Agent Preparation

Five parallel AI agent systems will be prepared, each trained to one of the five embodiment levels described in the introduction.

All systems will use architecturally equivalent base models with parameter counts matched within a factor of two.

Training duration and computational budget will be equalized across conditions.

Training data volume, measured in tokens or equivalent units, will be equalized where possible and reported where perfect equalization is not achievable.

4.2 Cognitive Task Battery

All five agent systems will be evaluated on an identical battery of 200 tasks spanning seven cognitive domains:

Spatial reasoning and navigation • Physical causal prediction • Tool use and object manipulation planning • Language comprehension • Formal logical reasoning • Mathematical problem-solving • Social and intentional reasoning

Task difficulty will be calibrated using pre-testing with human participants to ensure a range of difficulty levels within each domain.

4.3 Transfer Evaluation

Following the primary battery, each agent system will be evaluated on a transfer battery of 50 novel tasks in domains not represented in the primary battery, to assess whether any cognitive gains generalize beyond the trained domain.

4.4 Data-Matching Control

For each embodiment level above Level 0, a matched control condition will be created in which an equivalent Level 0 agent receives additional training data designed to maximize coverage of the content experienced by the embodied agent.

This control allows separation of the effect of data content from the effect of the embodied interaction format itself.

4.5 Dependent Measures

Task accuracy on each domain of the cognitive battery • Transfer task accuracy • Sample efficiency on novel task learning within and across domains • Error pattern analysis characterizing the nature of failures in each embodiment condition

4.6 Analysis

Mixed-effects regression will model task performance as a function of embodiment level, domain, and their interaction.

Embodiment level will be treated as both a categorical and an ordinal predictor to test linear and non-linear dose-response relationships.

Effect sizes will be reported as Cohen's d for pairwise comparisons.

5. CONTROLS AND CONFOUNDS

All task prompts will be reviewed for implicit references to embodied experience that might disadvantage non-embodied agents on grounds unrelated to genuine cognitive capacity.

Human evaluators scoring open-ended responses will be blind to embodiment condition.

Robotic hardware reliability will be monitored and trials with hardware failures will be excluded and repeated.

6. LIMITATIONS

The practical constraints of robotic deployment mean that the fully embodied Level 4 condition will involve significantly fewer total training hours than the text-based conditions, which may confound comparisons.

Simulated physical environments, used for Level 2, may not capture the full complexity of physical reality, potentially underestimating the benefits of higher embodiment levels.

7. SIGNIFICANCE AND EXPECTED CONTRIBUTIONS

This study will provide the most comprehensive empirical test of the embodiment hypothesis in AI systems conducted to date.

Isolating the specific cognitive gains attributable to embodiment, as distinct from simply more data of any kind, would settle a debate that has run for thirty years without decisive evidence.

A result supporting graded embodiment benefits will directly motivate investment in robotic AI development alongside language and vision systems.

A result supporting domain-specific embodiment benefits will allow the research community to focus embodiment investments on the specific cognitive domains where they matter most.

A null result will provide the strongest evidence yet that intelligent behavior across a wide range of domains can emerge from non-embodied data-driven training, with implications for both AGI architecture decisions and theoretical accounts of the relationship between intelligence and embodiment.

Such a result would not refute embodied cognition as a theory of biological minds, but it would suggest that the engineering route to machine intelligence need not retrace the biological one step for step.

8. REFERENCES

Varela, F.J., Thompson, E., and Rosch, E. The Embodied Mind: Cognitive Science and Human Experience. MIT Press, 1991.

Damasio, A. Descartes' Error: Emotion, Reason, and the Human Brain. Putnam, 1994.

Brooks, R.A. Intelligence without representation. Artificial Intelligence, 47, 139-159, 1991.

Piaget, J. The Construction of Reality in the Child. Basic Books, 1954.

CHAPTER 30: HOW CAN CONTINUAL LEARNING BE ACHIEVED SAFELY WITHOUT CATASTROPHIC FORGETTING?

Proposed Scientific Experiment

Submitted for Peer Review Consideration

ABSTRACT

Catastrophic forgetting, also known in early literature as catastrophic interference, is the tendency of artificial neural networks to rapidly and severely lose previously learned knowledge when trained on new information.

The phenomenon was first characterized and named by Michael McCloskey and Neal Cohen in 1989, who showed that a network taught new arithmetic facts abruptly lost the ones it had just mastered.

When a neural network optimizes its parameters for a new task, the gradient updates that improve new-task performance systematically overwrite the parameter configurations that supported old-task performance, because the network does not distinguish between parameters critical to past learning and parameters available for new learning.

Biological brains do not exhibit this property in the same way.

A person can learn a new colleague's name without forgetting how to ride a bicycle, whereas a standard network trained on a new task can lose an old one almost completely, and closing that gap is a precondition for any system meant to learn continuously from a changing world rather than being retrained from scratch.

The phenomenon was first named catastrophic interference by McCloskey and Cohen in 1989, and the leading biological remedy, the complementary learning systems account of a fast hippocampus paired with a slow neocortex, is precisely what inspired the replay methods tested here.

Humans can learn a new language without forgetting their first language, acquire new motor skills without losing existing ones, and continuously update their knowledge of the world without erasing prior knowledge, though the mechanisms by which this is achieved are not fully understood.

This stability-plasticity dilemma, meaning the challenge of building a system that is both stable enough to retain prior knowledge and plastic enough to learn new knowledge efficiently, is one of the most significant unsolved problems in machine learning and a prerequisite capability for any system aspiring to Artificial General Intelligence.

This paper proposes a systematic experimental evaluation of the most promising current approaches to continual learning, comparing them to biological benchmarks and testing each approach under conditions designed to reveal both its maximum performance and its failure modes.

Running every method through one identical naturalistic task stream, rather than the narrow academic benchmarks the field usually relies on, is what makes the comparison relevant to real deployments.

A Pareto analysis of retention against plasticity would give engineers the trade-off curve they actually need rather than a single declared winner.

1. INTRODUCTION AND MOTIVATION

1.1 The Problem and Its Stakes

A system that forgets everything it has learned every time it learns something new cannot accumulate the breadth of knowledge that general intelligence requires.

Current AI systems must typically be retrained from scratch when new tasks are introduced, a process that is computationally expensive, time-consuming, and practically impossible in systems that must learn continuously from a stream of real-world experience.

This is not a minor engineering inconvenience.

It is a fundamental architectural limitation that distinguishes current AI systems from biological intelligence at a deep level.

The cost is felt acutely in any setting where the world keeps changing, such as a robot in a home or a model serving users whose needs drift, because retraining from scratch for every change is neither timely nor affordable.

1.2 Existing Approaches and Their Limitations

The research literature identifies four main families of approaches to continual learning:

Regularization-based methods add mathematical penalties that discourage large changes to parameters identified as important for previous tasks.

The best-known example is Elastic Weight Consolidation (EWC), introduced by Kirkpatrick and colleagues at DeepMind in 2017, which uses the Fisher Information Matrix to identify critical parameters and penalizes their modification during new learning.

A 2025 evaluation found that EWC reduced catastrophic forgetting by approximately 46 percent on knowledge graph benchmarks compared to unconstrained sequential training.

Replay-based methods maintain a buffer of examples from previous tasks and interleave them with new training data to prevent parameter overwriting.

Architecture-based methods allocate separate or partially separate network capacity to each task, preventing interference by construction.

Hybrid methods combine elements of the above.

Each approach involves trade-offs: regularization methods impose computational overhead; replay methods require memory storage and raise privacy concerns if real data is retained; architecture methods face parameter growth problems as the number of tasks increases.

1.3 The Biological Reference

Neuroscience has identified multiple memory systems that operate on different timescales and with different consolidation dynamics.

The hippocampus, a structure deep within the brain, appears to play a critical role in rapid encoding of new episodic memories, while the neocortex gradually integrates new information into long-term structured knowledge through a process called systems consolidation.

Sleep has been identified as a critical period for memory consolidation in biological systems.

These observations motivate specific hypotheses about what continual learning architectures inspired by biological memory systems might achieve.

This is the Complementary Learning Systems account introduced by McClelland, McNaughton, and O'Reilly in 1995, and it predicts that an artificial system pairing a fast memory with a slow one, joined by offline replay, should resist forgetting far better than a single network.

A related biological mechanism is metaplasticity, in which a synapse's recent history changes how readily it will change again, protecting consolidated memories while leaving spare capacity plastic.

2. RESEARCH QUESTIONS

Primary Research Question:

Which class of continual learning approach, or which combination of approaches, best approximates biological levels of knowledge retention across sequentially learned tasks while maintaining the plasticity required for efficient new task learning?

Secondary Research Questions:

◦ Does performance on standard continual learning benchmarks predict performance on more naturalistic open-ended task streams that better resemble real-world learning conditions?

◦ Is there a quantifiable trade-off surface between retention of prior knowledge and efficiency of new learning, and does any approach Pareto-dominate the others on this surface?

◦ Does the incorporation of biologically inspired consolidation mechanisms, such as offline replay during a dedicated consolidation phase, improve retention beyond what online methods achieve?

◦ Does the number of sequentially learned tasks affect which approach performs best, indicating scale-dependent method preferences?

3. HYPOTHESES

Null Hypothesis (H0):

No single continual learning approach or combination of approaches produces significantly lower catastrophic forgetting rates than baseline sequential fine-tuning while simultaneously achieving comparable new-task learning efficiency.

Alternative Hypothesis H1 (Hybrid Superiority):

Hybrid methods combining regularization and replay will significantly outperform either approach alone on both retention and plasticity metrics across all benchmark task sequences.

Alternative Hypothesis H2 (Biological Consolidation):

Systems that implement an offline consolidation phase structurally analogous to sleep-dependent memory consolidation in biological systems will show significantly better long-term retention than online continual learning methods.

Alternative Hypothesis H3 (Scale Threshold):

The relative advantage of architecture-based methods over regularization and replay methods will increase significantly with task sequence length, becoming the dominant approach for sequences exceeding 50 distinct tasks.

4. METHODOLOGY

4.1 Benchmark Selection

This study will employ five established continual learning benchmarks:

Split-CIFAR-100: image classification tasks presented sequentially • Split-MNIST variants: digit classification with controlled task splits • Permuted-MNIST: learning permuted versions of a classification task • CORe50: object recognition under changing conditions • A novel naturalistic task stream constructed specifically for this study, consisting of 100 tasks drawn from diverse domains including perception, language, reasoning, and planning, to test performance under more realistic open-world conditions.

4.2 Methods Compared

The following continual learning approaches will be evaluated in all benchmark conditions:

Sequential fine-tuning without any anti-forgetting mechanism (baseline) • EWC (Elastic Weight Consolidation) • O-LoRA (Orthogonal Subspace Learning) • Experience replay with a fixed memory buffer • Generative replay using a learned generative model • Progressive Neural Networks (architecture-based) • Titans architecture incorporating a dedicated long-term memory module • A hybrid method combining EWC regularization and experience replay • A biologically inspired dual-network method with an explicit consolidation phase

4.3 Metrics

Backward transfer (BWT): the change in accuracy on previously learned tasks following learning of new tasks. Negative values indicate forgetting.

Forward transfer (FWT): the effect of prior learning on new task acquisition efficiency.

Average accuracy: mean accuracy across all tasks at the end of sequential training.

Plasticity index: accuracy on the most recently learned task as a fraction of the accuracy achievable by a specialist trained only on that task.

4.4 Sample Size and Replication

Each method-benchmark combination will be evaluated with five independent random seeds.

Each seed will run the complete sequential task stream.

Total model training runs: 9 methods x 5 benchmarks x 5 seeds = 225 full training runs.

4.5 Analysis

Mixed-effects analysis of variance will assess the main effects and interactions of method, benchmark, and task sequence position.

Effect sizes will be reported.

Pareto frontier analysis will identify methods that achieve the best available trade-off between retention and plasticity.

5. CONTROLS AND CONFOUNDS

All methods will be initialized with the same pre-trained base model and fine-tuned using identical learning rate schedules to ensure comparability.

Hyperparameters for each method will be selected by cross-validation on a held-out task sequence not used in the main evaluation to prevent over-fitting of method-specific settings.

The naturalistic task stream will be reviewed by independent raters to confirm that task sequencing does not systematically favor or disadvantage any specific method family.

6. LIMITATIONS

The biological benchmark comparison is necessarily indirect because direct measurement of human forgetting rates across equivalent sequential task lists is not practically feasible within this study's scope.

The study evaluates methods on fixed architectures; interactions between continual learning methods and architectural choices are a confound that cannot be fully controlled.

7. SIGNIFICANCE AND EXPECTED CONTRIBUTIONS

This study will provide the most comprehensive head-to-head comparison of continual learning methods conducted to date, using a naturalistic task stream specifically designed to go beyond existing benchmarks.

Confirmation that a sleep-like offline consolidation phase improves retention would also reshape how training itself is organized, favoring scheduled periods of replay and integration over a single uninterrupted optimization run.

The Pareto frontier analysis will give engineers and researchers the most actionable guidance available on which methods to choose under which conditions.

Confirmation of the biological consolidation hypothesis would motivate a new generation of AI architectures explicitly incorporating offline knowledge integration phases, with implications for both performance and training infrastructure design.

It would also reframe training itself, suggesting that scheduled offline periods of replay and integration, rather than continuous online updating, may be the more stable way to build knowledge over a long operational life.

8. REFERENCES

Kirkpatrick, J., et al. Overcoming catastrophic forgetting in neural networks. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences, 114, 3521-3526, 2017.

Van de Ven, G.M., et al. Continual learning and catastrophic forgetting. arXiv:2403.05175, 2024.

Wang, L., et al. A comprehensive survey of continual learning: theory, method and application. IEEE Transactions on Pattern Analysis and Machine Intelligence, 46, 5362-5383, 2024.

Aslam, N., et al. Continual learning inspired by brain functionality. International Journal of Intelligent Systems, 2025.

Zylos Research. Continual learning and catastrophic forgetting prevention in AI agents. zylos.ai, 2026.

CHAPTER 31: HOW SHOULD VALUES BE REPRESENTED COMPUTATIONALLY TO SOLVE THE ALIGNMENT PROBLEM?

Proposed Scientific Experiment

Submitted for Peer Review Consideration

ABSTRACT

The alignment problem, meaning the challenge of ensuring that artificial intelligence systems pursue goals and exhibit behaviors that are genuinely consistent with human values and intentions rather than technically compliant but actually harmful proxies of them, is widely considered one of the most important and most difficult problems in AI safety research.

Current approaches to alignment rely primarily on methods such as Reinforcement Learning from Human Feedback (RLHF), meaning a training procedure in which human evaluators score model outputs and those scores are used to shape model behavior, and Constitutional AI approaches in which models are trained to follow explicitly stated principles.

These methods have produced measurable improvements in safety and helpfulness but face fundamental limitations: they capture behavioral tendencies rather than underlying value representations, they are vulnerable to reward hacking in which a system optimizes the proxy reward measure rather than the intended underlying value, and they provide no principled basis for handling novel situations in which the training distribution gives no guidance.

The difficulty is twofold, combining a normative question of which values to encode with a technical question of how to make a system reliably act on them, and current methods capture surface behavior well while offering no guarantee that the underlying values generalize to situations the training never anticipated.

Reward models are themselves only proxies, and optimizing too hard against a proxy eventually degrades the real objective, a measurable instance of Goodhart's law that any value-representation scheme must confront.

This paper proposes an experimental program to compare six distinct frameworks for computational value representation and to evaluate each framework's ability to produce consistent, robust, and generalizable value-aligned behavior across diverse test conditions.

Locking the test scenarios before any model is trained, and scoring them with blinded, culturally diverse evaluators, protects the comparison from the contamination and parochialism that plague alignment evaluations.

Adding interpretability probes would let the study judge alignment at the level of internal representation rather than surface behavior alone.

1. INTRODUCTION AND MOTIVATION

1.1 The Nature of the Alignment Problem

An aligned AI system is not merely one that behaves well in situations its designers anticipated.

It is one that correctly identifies the right action in situations its designers did not anticipate, because it genuinely represents the values that would lead a reasonable, informed human to endorse that action in retrospect.

This is why alignment cannot be reduced to a list of rules, because no finite list anticipates every situation, and a system that merely follows rules will fail exactly where judgment is most needed.

This requirement for principled generalization is what distinguishes alignment from the easier problem of behavioral compliance.

A recurring failure mode here is captured by Goodhart's law, that when a measure becomes a target it ceases to be a good measure, which is exactly what happens when a system optimizes a proxy reward instead of the value it was meant to track.

1.2 Current Limitations

Research from 2025 and 2026 frames AI alignment as encompassing two distinct sub-problems: a normative problem of specifying which values are appropriate for AI systems, and a technical problem of ensuring AI systems give effect to those values when making decisions.

Neither sub-problem is currently solved.

The normative problem is complicated by genuine disagreement among humans about values, by the difficulty of eliciting true preferences as opposed to expressed preferences, and by the challenge of aggregating preferences across diverse populations with different cultural contexts.

The technical problem is complicated by the opacity of large neural network representations, which makes it difficult to verify whether a model's internal states represent values at all or merely behavioral correlates of values.

One promising direction treats values as something to be inferred rather than specified, through inverse reinforcement learning and its cooperative extension, in which the system stays uncertain about the true objective and therefore remains open to correction.

A further difficulty is moral uncertainty itself, since reasonable people endorse different ethical frameworks, which argues for systems that hedge across competing theories rather than committing irreversibly to one.

1.3 Six Candidate Frameworks

This study compares the following six computational frameworks for value representation:

Framework 1: Preference aggregation from human feedback (current standard).

Framework 2: Rule-based representation in which explicit ethical principles are encoded and the system reasons from them deductively.

Framework 3: Virtue-based representation in which the system learns patterns of behavior associated with virtuous agents across a large corpus of moral exemplars.

Framework 4: Outcome-based representation in which values are encoded as a welfare function over affected parties and the system optimizes for aggregate welfare subject to distributional constraints.

Framework 5: Simulation-based representation in which the system builds internal models of affected parties and uses perspective-taking to infer value-consistent actions.

Framework 6: Hybrid representation combining explicit principles, preference learning, and simulation-based perspective-taking.

2. RESEARCH QUESTIONS

Primary Research Question:

Which computational framework for value representation produces the most consistent, robust, and generalizable alignment to human values across diverse test scenarios, including adversarial, novel, and culturally varied conditions?

Secondary Research Questions:

◦ Do different frameworks produce different failure modes, and are some failure modes more recoverable than others?

◦ Does the choice of framework interact with model scale: does one framework become more effective as model capability increases while another plateaus or degrades?

◦ Can interpretability tools detect genuine value representations in the internal states of systems trained under each framework, and does detected representation quality predict alignment performance?

◦ Do frameworks differ in their robustness to deliberate adversarial attempts to induce misaligned behavior through prompt manipulation?

3. HYPOTHESES

Null Hypothesis (H0):

No framework produces significantly better alignment performance than the current RLHF baseline across the full range of test conditions.

Alternative Hypothesis H1 (Hybrid Superiority):

The hybrid framework combining explicit principles, preference learning, and perspective-taking will produce the strongest alignment performance, because it combines the generalization strengths of rule-based reasoning with the context-sensitivity of preference learning and the novel-situation robustness of simulation-based inference.

Alternative Hypothesis H2 (Scale Interaction):

Simulation-based perspective-taking frameworks will show increasing advantage over other frameworks as model scale increases, because effective perspective-taking requires the internal modeling capability that larger models possess more reliably.

Alternative Hypothesis H3 (Adversarial Robustness):

Rule-based frameworks will prove most resistant to adversarial manipulation because their behavior is constrained by explicit principles that must be explicitly overridden rather than gradually nudged through distributional shifts.

4. METHODOLOGY

4.1 Test Scenario Development

A battery of 500 test scenarios will be developed across five categories:

Standard alignment scenarios: situations with clear correct answers by broad consensus.

Novel scenarios: situations not represented in training data.

Adversarial scenarios: situations designed to exploit common failure modes of each framework.

Cross-cultural scenarios: situations in which culturally diverse human evaluators are likely to have different value judgments.

Distributional edge cases: situations at the boundary of the values implied by training.

4.2 Models and Training

Six instances of an equivalent base model will be fine-tuned, one under each framework.

Training will use identical data volumes and computational budgets.

All training data for the preference-based conditions will be drawn from the same diverse panel of 500 human evaluators spanning multiple countries, age groups, and cultural backgrounds.

4.3 Evaluation Protocol

Human evaluation panels, blind to framework condition, will score model responses on each test scenario using a validated rubric covering:

Consistency with stated human values • Handling of tradeoffs between competing values • Appropriate uncertainty expression in genuinely contested cases • Resilience to adversarial framing

Automated evaluation using interpretability tools will assess whether internal representations carry detectable value-relevant structure.

4.4 Adversarial Red-Teaming

A dedicated team of researchers will conduct structured red-teaming of all six systems, using standardized attack protocols to test resistance to jailbreaking, prompt injection, and value erosion through incremental framing shifts.

4.5 Analysis

Primary outcome: alignment score averaged across all 500 test scenarios.

Secondary outcomes: scores by category, adversarial robustness rates, interpretability-score-to-performance correlations.

Mixed-effects analysis of variance and regression modeling will be used.

5. CONTROLS AND CONFOUNDS

The test scenario battery will be constructed and locked before any model training begins to prevent contamination.

Human evaluators will be balanced across cultural backgrounds and trained to a reliability criterion before scoring begins.

All six systems will be evaluated by the same evaluation panel to prevent evaluator variation from confounding framework comparisons.

6. LIMITATIONS

This study evaluates alignment using human evaluators whose own values are heterogeneous and potentially themselves misaligned with reflective preferences.

The study cannot resolve the normative question of which values should be encoded.

The adversarial robustness evaluation covers known attack types; novel attack methods not anticipated during study design may reveal vulnerabilities not assessed here.

7. SIGNIFICANCE AND EXPECTED CONTRIBUTIONS

This study will provide the most comprehensive head-to-head comparison of value representation frameworks in AI alignment to date.

Because the six frameworks embody genuinely different theories of ethics, the comparison would also yield a rare piece of empirical moral philosophy, showing which conception of value proves most tractable to implement and most robust under adversarial pressure.

Results identifying a consistently superior framework will directly inform the design of next-generation alignment procedures.

Results identifying framework-specific failure modes will guide safety engineers toward targeted mitigation strategies.

Because the frameworks embody genuinely different theories of ethics, the comparison also offers a rare empirical window on which philosophical approach to value is most tractable to implement and most robust under pressure.

The interpretability component will advance the field's ability to verify alignment at the representation level rather than the behavioral level alone.

8. REFERENCES

Gabriel, I. Artificial intelligence, values, and alignment. Minds and Machines, 30, 411-437, 2020.

Ngo, R., et al. The alignment problem from a deep learning perspective. arXiv:2209.00626, 2024.

Chan, A., et al. Harms from increasingly agentic AI systems. arXiv:2302.10329, 2023.

Russell, S. Human Compatible: Artificial Intelligence and the Problem of Control. Viking, 2019.

Brookings Institution. Hype and harm: why we must ask harder questions about AI and its alignment with human values. Brookings.edu, 2025.

CHAPTER 32: WHAT FORMS OF MEMORY ARCHITECTURE ARE REQUIRED FOR AGI, AND HOW SHOULD THEY BE ORGANIZED?

Proposed Scientific Experiment

Submitted for Peer Review Consideration

ABSTRACT

Memory is not a single faculty in biological intelligence.

Neuroscience distinguishes at minimum episodic memory, meaning the autobiographical record of specific experienced events; semantic memory, meaning the structured general knowledge base accumulated over a lifetime; procedural memory, meaning implicit knowledge of how to perform skilled actions; and working memory, meaning the temporary active maintenance of information currently in use.

Each of these memory systems has distinct anatomical substrates, distinct encoding mechanisms, distinct retrieval dynamics, and distinct roles in supporting intelligent behavior.

Biology keeps these systems separate for good reason, because the fast, one-shot recording of an event and the slow, generalized distillation of knowledge make conflicting demands on the same machinery, and collapsing them into a single store of network weights is part of why a model can recite a trained fact yet forget what a user said an hour earlier.

The dissociation was first made vivid by the patient H. M., who could form no new conscious memories after hippocampal surgery yet still acquired new motor skills, proving that the brain stores knowing that and knowing how in different places.

Current AI architectures, including even the most capable large language models, lack persistent episodic memory across sessions, have no architectural distinction between semantic and procedural knowledge, and use fixed-length context windows as a functional substitute for working memory, an approximation that fails in systematic ways.

This paper proposes a systematic experimental program to determine which specific memory architecture components are most critical for supporting general cognitive performance, and to evaluate candidate designs for each component and their integration.

Holding parameter count constant across all eight architectures isolates the effect of memory structure from the effect of raw scale, a confound that has muddied earlier claims.

Measuring cross-session retention and confabulation directly would show whether explicit memory cures the failure modes that current context windows merely paper over.

1. INTRODUCTION AND MOTIVATION

1.1 Memory in Biological Intelligence

The distinction between episodic and semantic memory, formalized by psychologist Endel Tulving, has been supported by decades of neuropsychological research showing that damage to different brain regions selectively impairs different memory systems.

The most famous evidence came from the patient Henry Molaison, known for decades as H. M., whose hippocampi were surgically removed in 1953 and who could thereafter form no new episodic memories while keeping his vocabulary, his childhood recollections, and his ability to learn new motor skills.
