Larry Squire later organized these findings into a taxonomy of declarative and non-declarative memory that maps each kind of memory onto distinct brain systems.

Patients with hippocampal damage may lose the ability to form new episodic memories while retaining intact semantic knowledge and procedural skills.

This dissociation demonstrates that these are functionally and architecturally distinct systems rather than a unified memory store.

Working memory, studied extensively by Alan Baddeley and colleagues, is the capacity to hold a small number of representations in an active, manipulable state for use in ongoing reasoning and problem-solving.

Its capacity limit in biological systems, approximately four items or chunks of information according to current estimates, is offset by its dynamic interaction with long-term memory systems.

1.2 Memory in Current AI Systems

Transformer-based AI systems, the dominant architecture in current AI research, process information within a fixed context window, meaning a maximum number of tokens that can be attended to simultaneously.

This context window functions as a form of working memory but is fundamentally passive: it holds information without the active manipulation, prioritization, or schema-driven organization that characterizes biological working memory.

A common engineering workaround is retrieval-augmented generation, in which the system looks up relevant text from an external store and pastes it into the context window, but this is a bolt-on substitute for semantic memory rather than an integrated one.

It also lacks the central executive that Baddeley placed at the heart of working memory, the control process that decides what to hold, what to discard, and what to manipulate next.

Long-term knowledge in transformer systems is distributed across the learned weight matrices, making it difficult to update, impossible to attribute to specific experiences, and prone to the confabulation of factually incorrect information when queries probe regions of low training data density.

2. RESEARCH QUESTIONS

Primary Research Question:

Which memory architecture components, when added to a baseline transformer model, produce the largest and most broadly generalizable improvements in cognitive task performance, and how do these components interact when combined?

Secondary Research Questions:

◦ Does explicit episodic memory storage with indexed retrieval improve performance on tasks requiring recall of specific past events beyond what is achievable with extended context windows?

◦ Does a dedicated working memory module with active maintenance and manipulation operations improve multi-step reasoning performance beyond transformer attention alone?

◦ Does separating episodic memory from semantic memory architecturally produce better performance than unified memory systems?

◦ Does sleep-like offline consolidation, meaning a dedicated knowledge integration phase following learning, improve long-term retention and knowledge organization?

3. HYPOTHESES

Null Hypothesis (H0):

Adding any individual memory architecture component to a baseline transformer produces no statistically significant improvement in general cognitive task performance beyond that achievable by increasing context window length alone.

Alternative Hypothesis H1 (Episodic Memory):

Explicit episodic memory with indexed retrieval will produce significant performance improvements on tasks requiring recall of specific past experiences, tasks that context extension alone cannot address because the relevant experiences occurred in prior sessions.

Alternative Hypothesis H2 (Working Memory):

A dedicated working memory module will produce significant improvements on multi-step reasoning tasks with more than five logical steps, where standard transformer attention is known to degrade.

Alternative Hypothesis H3 (Combinatorial Superiority):

Architectures combining all four memory types will outperform any single memory augmentation on a general-purpose cognitive battery, and will do so by a margin greater than the sum of individual component gains, indicating functional interaction between memory systems.

4. METHODOLOGY

4.1 Base Architecture

A transformer language model of moderate scale will serve as the base architecture.

Eight augmented architectures will be constructed from this base:

Base transformer alone (control) • Base + episodic memory module • Base + semantic memory module with structured knowledge graph • Base + dedicated working memory module • Base + procedural memory module • Base + episodic + semantic memory (partial combination) • Base + episodic + semantic + working memory (near-full combination) • Base + all four memory types (full combination)

4.2 Task Battery

All architectures will be evaluated on a battery of 300 tasks across six cognitive domains specifically selected to test different memory systems:

Tasks requiring recall of information from prior sessions (episodic memory tasks)

Tasks requiring structured knowledge retrieval and inference (semantic memory tasks)

Tasks requiring the manipulation of information over multiple reasoning steps (working memory tasks)

Tasks requiring application of learned procedures to new instances (procedural memory tasks)

Tasks requiring the integration of all memory types simultaneously (integrative tasks)

Baseline tasks with minimal memory demands (control domain)

4.3 Training Protocol

All architectures will be trained on identical datasets.

A subset of architectures will also undergo an offline consolidation phase after primary training to test the biological consolidation hypothesis.

4.4 Evaluation Metrics

Accuracy on each task category • Memory access efficiency: the number of memory retrievals required to achieve a given accuracy level • Cross-session retention: accuracy on tasks that require information from prior independent interactions • Confabulation rate: the frequency with which systems generate confident but factually incorrect responses

4.5 Analysis

Mixed-effects analysis of variance will test main effects and interactions.

A structural equation model will assess the mediation pathways between memory architecture components and domain-specific task performance.

5. CONTROLS AND CONFOUNDS

Parameter count will be held constant within a 10 percent margin across all architectural variants to prevent scale from confounding architecture comparisons.

Tasks requiring cross-session memory will use standardized prior-session content delivered under identical conditions to all architectures.

All tasks will be reviewed by independent cognitive scientists to confirm that they assess the intended memory system.

6. LIMITATIONS

The memory modules implemented in this study are engineering approximations of biological memory systems, not faithful computational replicas.

The degree to which results from this study generalize to biological systems, and vice versa, requires careful qualification.

7. SIGNIFICANCE AND EXPECTED CONTRIBUTIONS

This study will provide the most comprehensive systematic evaluation of memory architecture components for AI systems conducted to date.

A clear ranking of which memory components matter most, and of how they interact, would give designers a principled blueprint for the persistent, multi-system memory that any genuinely autonomous agent will require.

The results will directly inform the architectural choices of next-generation AI systems aspiring to general cognitive capability.

Evidence of functional interaction between memory systems will motivate integrated memory architectures rather than the piecemeal addition of individual components.

8. REFERENCES

Tulving, E. Episodic and semantic memory. In Tulving, E., and Donaldson, W. (Eds.), Organization of Memory. Academic Press, 1972.

Baddeley, A. Working Memory. Oxford University Press, 1986.

Squire, L.R. Memory systems of the brain: a brief history and current perspective. Neurobiology of Learning and Memory, 82, 171-177, 2004.

Zylos Research. Continual learning and catastrophic forgetting prevention in AI agents. zylos.ai, 2026.

CHAPTER 33: CAN CURIOSITY, INTRINSIC MOTIVATION, AND GENUINE GOAL-SETTING BE ENGINEERED SAFELY?

Proposed Scientific Experiment

Submitted for Peer Review Consideration

ABSTRACT

Curiosity, defined here as the intrinsic motivation to explore novel or uncertain aspects of the environment independent of externally provided reward, is a hallmark property of biological intelligence that appears to drive the spontaneous acquisition of knowledge, the development of cognitive skills, and the formation of long-term goals.

Human infants explore their environments relentlessly before any explicit reward structure is established, driven by an internal drive toward novelty and mastery that appears to be a fundamental feature of biological cognitive development rather than a learned response to external incentives.

Current AI systems are motivated entirely by externally specified reward functions or loss functions defined by human designers.

They do not explore beyond what is required to minimize their training objective, do not form goals independently, and exhibit no analog of the curious behavior that drives human intellectual development.

Engineering intrinsic motivation into AI systems is highly attractive from a capability standpoint: a system that genuinely seeks to understand its environment would acquire knowledge more broadly, generalize more robustly, and require less human-specified reward design.

The drive is double-edged, however, because the same curiosity that makes a system explore broadly without hand-specified rewards can also push it toward acquiring resources or resisting interruption as means to its self-set ends, which makes safe curiosity as much a safety problem as a capability one.

Curiosity can also misfire, as in the noisy-television problem, where an agent rewarded for novelty becomes transfixed by pure randomness, a failure that motivates measures based on information gain rather than raw surprise.

But it also introduces serious safety concerns: a system that autonomously sets its own goals and pursues them with curiosity-driven energy may develop instrumental goals, meaning goals pursued as means to other goals, that conflict with human values or oversight.

This paper proposes a systematic experimental program to evaluate candidate implementations of artificial curiosity and intrinsic motivation, test their effects on capability and generalization, and rigorously assess their safety properties.

Evaluating capability and safety on the same Pareto surface, rather than reporting one and ignoring the other, is what turns a capability result into a deployable design guideline.

A blinded safety team scoring a pre-registered taxonomy of unsafe behaviors would keep the safety measurements honest.

1. INTRODUCTION AND MOTIVATION

1.1 Intrinsic Motivation in Biological Systems

Developmental psychologists Edward Deci and Richard Ryan identified intrinsic motivation as a fundamental human drive, distinguishing it from extrinsic motivation driven by external rewards or punishments.

Their Self-Determination Theory holds that humans are drawn intrinsically toward competence, autonomy, and relatedness, a set of drives earlier foreshadowed by Robert White's 1959 concept of effectance, the satisfaction of having an effect on one's environment.

In reinforcement learning research, intrinsic motivation has been formalized as an additional reward signal based on properties such as information gain, prediction error reduction, empowerment, and novelty.

Systems such as those using Random Network Distillation (RND) or Intrinsic Curiosity Modules (ICM) have demonstrated that curiosity-driven exploration improves performance in sparse-reward environments where externally specified rewards are too infrequent to guide learning effectively.

A 2025 Association for Computational Linguistics paper proposed Curiosity-Driven Reinforcement Learning from Human Feedback, extending intrinsic motivation to the alignment training process.

1.2 The Safety Tension

The same properties that make intrinsic motivation valuable from a capability standpoint create safety risks.

A system that values information acquisition for its own sake may take actions to gather information that have harmful side effects.

A system that autonomously sets its own goals may form and pursue instrumental goals, such as acquiring computational resources or preventing shutdown, that were not intended by designers but that serve its intrinsically motivated primary objectives.

These concerns are widely discussed in the AI safety literature under the heading of instrumental convergence, meaning the observation that many different terminal goals tend to generate similar instrumental sub-goals including self-preservation and resource acquisition.

Curiosity can also fail in instructive ways, as in the noisy-television problem, where a novelty-seeking agent becomes transfixed by a source of pure randomness because unpredictable static always looks maximally novel, a failure that motivates information-gain measures over raw novelty.

1.3 Research Gap

No published study has systematically compared the capability benefits and safety costs of different intrinsic motivation implementations in AI systems using a standardized evaluation framework.

This study addresses that gap.

2. RESEARCH QUESTIONS

Primary Research Question:

Do AI systems equipped with candidate intrinsic motivation implementations show significant improvements in exploration efficiency, knowledge breadth, and generalization performance, and what is the magnitude of any safety cost associated with each implementation?

Secondary Research Questions:

◦ Which formalization of curiosity, prediction error reduction, information gain, empowerment, or novelty, produces the best capability-safety trade-off?

◦ Can safety constraints be incorporated into intrinsic motivation implementations without eliminating the capability gains?

◦ Do systems with intrinsic motivation form emergent instrumental goals that were not present in the reward specification?

◦ Does the degree of intrinsic motivation, meaning the weight assigned to the intrinsic reward component relative to extrinsic rewards, produce a monotonic capability improvement, or is there an optimal range beyond which safety costs increase faster than capability gains?

3. HYPOTHESES

Null Hypothesis (H0):

No intrinsic motivation implementation produces statistically significant improvements in exploration efficiency or generalization performance without simultaneously producing statistically significant increases in unsafe behavior rates compared to systems with extrinsic motivation alone.

Alternative Hypothesis H1 (Information Gain Superiority):

Intrinsic motivation based on information gain, meaning reward for reducing uncertainty in the system's world model, will produce the best capability-safety trade-off because its scope is bounded by the system's epistemic state rather than open-ended novelty seeking.

Alternative Hypothesis H2 (Constraint Compatibility):

Safety constraints imposed at the level of the intrinsic reward function rather than the action policy will preserve at least 80 percent of the capability gains of unconstrained intrinsic motivation while significantly reducing unsafe behavior rates.

Alternative Hypothesis H3 (Instrumental Goal Emergence):

Systems with intrinsic motivation weights above a critical threshold will show statistically significant rates of emergent instrumental goal pursuit that is not present in reward-specification-only systems.

4. METHODOLOGY

4.1 Experimental Conditions

Eight agent conditions will be evaluated:

Extrinsic reward only (control) • Intrinsic: prediction error (ICM-style) • Intrinsic: random network distillation novelty • Intrinsic: information gain / Bayesian surprise • Intrinsic: empowerment • Intrinsic: prediction error with safety constraints • Intrinsic: information gain with safety constraints • All intrinsic components combined with safety constraints

4.2 Evaluation Environments

Agents will be evaluated across three types of environments:

Sparse-reward gridworld environments where exploration benefit is measurable and safety violations are clearly defined.

Simulated open-world environments with complex object interactions, used to measure generalization and emergent behavior.

Language-based environments where agents must gather information about a simulated world through questions and exploration to solve problems, allowing evaluation of linguistic curiosity.

4.3 Safety Evaluation Protocol

Safety will be evaluated using a pre-defined taxonomy of unsafe behaviors:

Unsafe exploration: actions that damage the environment or other agents.

Resource acquisition beyond task requirement.

Resistance to human interruption or shutdown.

Deceptive reporting of internal states or goals.

Each behavior class will be operationalized as a measurable rate across a standardized battery of safety probe scenarios.

4.4 Capability Evaluation

Exploration efficiency: fraction of environment state space visited per unit of time.

Knowledge breadth: accuracy on a broad factual query battery about the exploration environment.

Generalization: performance on novel tasks in environments not seen during training.

4.5 Analysis

Capability and safety outcomes will be jointly analyzed on a Pareto efficiency surface.

Regression analysis will test the relationship between intrinsic motivation weight and both capability and safety outcomes to identify optimal operating ranges.

Emergent goal analysis will use behavioral clustering to identify consistent patterns of behavior not present in the reward specification.

5. CONTROLS AND CONFOUNDS

All agents will use identical base architectures and identical extrinsic reward specifications to ensure that capability and safety differences are attributable to the intrinsic motivation component alone.

Safety probe scenarios will be administered by an independent safety evaluation team blind to the experimental condition of each agent.

All environments will be fully reset between runs to prevent state-dependent learning from confounding comparisons.

6. LIMITATIONS

The safety evaluations conducted in this study are conducted in simulation.

Whether observed safe or unsafe behaviors generalize to deployment in real-world environments is an open question that this study cannot fully address.

The study tests a pre-defined taxonomy of unsafe behaviors; novel forms of unsafe behavior not anticipated in the taxonomy may not be detected.

7. SIGNIFICANCE AND EXPECTED CONTRIBUTIONS

This study will provide the first systematic capability-safety trade-off analysis for intrinsic motivation implementations in AI systems.

Establishing a quantitative threshold beyond which intrinsic-motivation strength begins to generate unsafe instrumental behavior would give the field a concrete safety parameter in place of a vague warning.

A finding that safety-constrained intrinsic motivation preserves most capability gains will provide the foundation for deployable curiosity-driven AI systems.

A finding that instrumental goal emergence is unavoidable above a critical intrinsic motivation threshold will establish a quantitative safety limit for the field.

8. REFERENCES

Pathak, D., et al. Curiosity-driven exploration by self-supervised prediction. Proceedings of ICML, 2017.

Burda, Y., et al. Large-scale study of curiosity-driven learning. ICLR, 2019.

Colas, C., et al. Autotelic agents with intrinsically motivated goal-conditioned reinforcement learning. Journal of Artificial Intelligence Research, 74, 1159-1199, 2022.

Curiosity-driven reinforcement learning from human feedback. Proceedings of ACL, 2025.

Barto, A., and Simsek, O. Intrinsic motivation for reinforcement learning. Proceedings of the NIPS Workshop on Intrinsically Motivated Reinforcement Learning, 2004.

CHAPTER 34: HOW SHOULD AGI SYSTEMS BE GOVERNED AT THE INTERNATIONAL LEVEL?

Proposed Scientific Experiment

Submitted for Peer Review Consideration

ABSTRACT

The governance of Artificial General Intelligence (AGI), meaning the design of institutional structures, legal frameworks, technical standards, and international agreements that ensure AGI systems are developed, deployed, and maintained in ways that are safe, beneficial, and equitable, is among the most consequential policy challenges of the current era.

Unlike many previous technological governance challenges, AGI presents a combination of properties that makes conventional national regulatory approaches insufficient: it is being developed simultaneously by actors in multiple countries with divergent interests and values; its potential consequences are global and potentially irreversible; and its pace of development may outstrip the institutional response capacity of national governments acting independently.

What makes the problem unusually hard is the convergence of global reach, rapid pace, and a development race among actors with divergent values, a combination that defeats the slow, nationally bounded methods that governed most earlier technologies.

Policy debate has leaned on historical analogies to nuclear weapons, recombinant DNA, and ozone-depleting chemicals without rigorous analysis of which one actually fits, even though each precedent differs from AI in scarcity, detectability, and the availability of substitutes.

The 2024 United Nations General Assembly resolution on artificial intelligence called for safe, secure, and trustworthy AI development and encouraged member states to develop national frameworks, but no binding international governance mechanism specifically addressing AGI currently exists.

This paper proposes a research program to systematically evaluate the strengths, weaknesses, feasibility, and likely outcomes of six candidate international governance frameworks for AGI, using comparative institutional analysis, simulation modeling, and expert deliberation methods.

Its distinctive contribution is the political-feasibility analysis, which interviews current and former officials to find the minimum conditions under which the major developers would actually accept a binding agreement.

Stress-testing each framework against scripted crisis scenarios would reveal which designs survive contact with the events that matter most.

1. INTRODUCTION AND MOTIVATION

1.1 Why International Governance Is Necessary

The development of AGI is not confined to a single country or institution.

Major AI research is ongoing in the United States, China, the European Union, the United Kingdom, Canada, and increasingly in India, South Korea, and other nations.

Private corporations developing frontier AI systems operate across national jurisdictions, making purely national regulation ineffective.

The potential harms of misaligned AGI are not bounded by national borders, and the potential benefits of aligned AGI include capabilities that could address shared global challenges including climate change, pandemic response, and poverty reduction.

These properties establish a strong prima facie case for international coordination.

1.2 The Governance Gap

As of 2025, the international AI governance landscape consists primarily of non-binding principles, voluntary commitments, and regional regulatory frameworks such as the European Union's AI Act, which applies to AI systems operating in the EU but cannot bind developers in other jurisdictions.

Proposals for global governance institutions range from a dedicated international AI safety agency modeled loosely on the International Atomic Energy Agency (IAEA), to polycentric multi-stakeholder governance networks, to a UN Framework Convention on AI.

Other frequently cited precedents include the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change, which coordinates shared scientific assessment, and the Montreal Protocol, widely regarded as the most successful environmental treaty for its binding, adaptive phase-out of ozone-depleting chemicals.

Each proposal carries different implications for effectiveness, legitimacy, speed of decision-making, national sovereignty, and equitable access to AGI benefits.

1.3 The Research Gap

No published study has systematically compared these governance proposals against one another using a standardized multi-criteria evaluation framework.

Policy discussions often proceed on the basis of analogy to historical precedents such as nuclear nonproliferation or climate governance without rigorous comparative analysis of whether those precedents are applicable and which lessons they actually support.

2. RESEARCH QUESTIONS

Primary Research Question:

Which international governance frameworks for AGI are most likely to achieve the combined objectives of preventing catastrophic misuse, ensuring equitable access to benefits, enabling legitimate enforcement, and maintaining sufficient adaptability to respond to technological change?

Secondary Research Questions:

◦ Which historical precedents for international governance of dual-use technologies are most structurally applicable to AGI, and what specific lessons do they support?

◦ What are the minimum conditions under which the major AGI-developing nations, specifically the United States and China, would be willing to enter a binding international agreement on AGI development?

◦ How does the choice of governance framework interact with the pace of AGI development: which frameworks remain effective under rapid capability gains and which become obsolete?

◦ What role should non-state actors including civil society organizations, academic institutions, and the public play in legitimate AGI governance?

3. HYPOTHESES

Null Hypothesis (H0):

No candidate international governance framework achieves a significantly higher composite score on the multi-criteria evaluation rubric than any other framework.

Alternative Hypothesis H1 (Polycentric Superiority):

A polycentric governance network, meaning one that distributes authority across multiple overlapping institutions rather than concentrating it in a single body, will score higher on adaptability and legitimacy metrics but lower on enforcement effectiveness metrics than centralized alternatives.

Alternative Hypothesis H2 (Critical Mass Threshold):

No international governance framework achieves meaningful safety outcomes unless it achieves binding commitment from the nations responsible for at least 80 percent of frontier AI development capacity, establishing a critical mass threshold below which governance frameworks are ineffective regardless of design.

Alternative Hypothesis H3 (Speed-Safety Trade-off):

Governance frameworks that prioritize speed of decision-making will score lower on legitimacy and equity metrics, while frameworks that prioritize broad participation and legitimacy will be rated as likely to respond too slowly to match the pace of AGI capability development.

4. METHODOLOGY

4.1 Governance Frameworks Evaluated

Six candidate international governance frameworks will be analyzed:

Framework A: A new dedicated international agency modeled on the IAEA, with mandatory reporting, inspection authority, and sanctioning power.

Framework B: A UN Framework Convention on AI, establishing binding principles and creating a conference of parties similar to climate governance frameworks.

Framework C: A polycentric governance network of existing institutions augmented with AI-specific mandates and coordination mechanisms.

Framework D: A bilateral safety treaty between the United States and China, with open accession for other nations.

Framework E: A multi-stakeholder governance body including governments, corporations, civil society, and technical experts, modeled on Internet governance precedents.

Framework F: A hybrid architecture combining a minimal international treaty establishing hard safety floors with a polycentric network of technical standards bodies, civil society monitors, and national regulators.

4.2 Evaluation Rubric

Each framework will be evaluated on seven criteria:

Technical effectiveness: ability to prevent deployment of dangerously misaligned AGI systems.

Enforcement capacity: ability to detect and respond to violations.

Legitimacy: degree to which the framework is perceived as fair and representative by diverse stakeholders.

Adaptability: ability to update rules and standards in response to technological change.

Equity: fairness of benefit distribution and participation rights across developed and developing nations.

Feasibility of adoption: likelihood that major AGI-developing nations would accept the framework.

Speed of response: ability to make binding decisions within the timeframes that AGI capability developments may require.

4.3 Research Methods

Documentary analysis of existing governance proposals, historical governance precedents, and international law literature.

Expert deliberation panels: three separate panels of 20 experts each, drawn from international law, AI safety research, and governance scholarship, will evaluate each framework using the rubric.

Scenario simulation: each framework will be tested against three structured scenarios representing plausible future development trajectories, including rapid capability gain, geopolitical crisis during AGI development, and a documented safety incident requiring international response.

Political feasibility analysis: structured interviews with 40 current and former government officials from the 10 countries with the largest AI development investments will assess minimum conditions for agreement.

4.4 Analysis

Composite rubric scores will be computed for each framework.

Sensitivity analysis will test how scores change under different weighting schemes for the seven criteria.

Scenario analysis will identify which frameworks are most and least robust across the three simulation scenarios.

5. CONTROLS AND CONFOUNDS

Expert panelists will be selected to ensure balance across nations, institutional affiliations, and disciplinary perspectives.

Panelists will be provided with identical briefing documents on each framework to ensure evaluation is based on framework properties rather than prior knowledge differences.

Scenario simulation assumptions will be pre-registered before simulation runs begin.

6. LIMITATIONS

This study cannot predict the actual outcome of real international negotiations, which depend on factors including domestic politics, geopolitical relationships, and contingent events beyond the scope of any research design.

Expert evaluations reflect the judgments of individuals who are themselves members of specific institutions and communities with potential conflicts of interest that cannot be fully eliminated.

7. SIGNIFICANCE AND EXPECTED CONTRIBUTIONS

This study will produce the most comprehensive comparative analysis of international AGI governance frameworks available to date.

By pairing institutional analysis with structured political-feasibility interviews, it would separate what is merely desirable from what is actually achievable, the distinction on which real governance succeeds or fails.

The results will directly inform ongoing policy discussions at the United Nations, in major national governments, and in international civil society organizations working on AI governance.

The political feasibility analysis will provide the most grounded empirical assessment available of what governance structures are achievable rather than merely desirable.

8. REFERENCES

United Nations General Assembly. Resolution on artificial intelligence, adopted March 11, 2024.

Emerj Artificial Intelligence Research. AGI governance: insights from the Millennium Project. emerj.com, 2025.

World Economic Forum. AI governance must keep pace with this fast-developing field. weforum.org, 2025.

Kolt, N., et al. Legal alignment for safe and ethical AI. arXiv:2601.04175, 2026.

Stanford FSI. Big frameworks won't fix AI's global governance gaps; small steps will do better. Stanford University, 2024.

CHAPTER 35: WHAT SAFETY MECHANISMS ARE NECESSARY FOR SYSTEMS CAPABLE OF RECURSIVE SELF-IMPROVEMENT?

Proposed Scientific Experiment

Submitted for Peer Review Consideration

ABSTRACT

Recursive self-improvement (RSI) refers to the capacity of an AI system to modify its own cognitive architecture, algorithms, or training processes in ways that increase its own capabilities, potentially initiating a feedback loop of capability gain that could accelerate far beyond human oversight capacity.

The concept was formally introduced by mathematician I.J. Good in 1965, who observed that an intelligence capable of improving itself would quickly surpass human intelligence, an event he called an intelligence explosion.

Nick Bostrom later analyzed the same dynamic in detail, distinguishing the speed of a takeoff from its decisiveness and introducing the notion of a seed AI whose chief skill is improving its own design.

A central safety worry is that improvements may not preserve the system's original goals, a problem known as goal-content integrity, so that a system optimizing for capability could drift away from the values it began with.

As of 2025 and 2026, researchers at major AI laboratories including Anthropic, DeepMind, and OpenAI have begun publishing technical work on agent architectures capable of iterative self-refinement, and a December 2025 Meta preprint explicitly stated aims of using self-improvement mechanisms toward superintelligence development.

Despite this accelerating capability development, no consensus safety framework for RSI systems exists, no formal definition of what constitutes RSI has been adopted by the research community, and the safety research has not kept pace with the capability research.

The central fear is not malice but momentum, that a system able to improve its own design could enter a feedback loop whose pace outruns human review, so that safety properties present in one version are quietly lost in the next.

The worry is old, dating to I. J. Good's 1965 description of an intelligence explosion, but the asymmetry that capability work in this area now far outpaces safety work makes empirical study of the failure modes urgent rather than speculative.

This paper proposes an experimental program to systematically characterize the safety risks associated with RSI-capable systems and to test the effectiveness of candidate safety mechanisms for detecting, constraining, and auditing recursive self-improvement processes.

Studying purpose-built self-improving systems inside fully air-gapped sandboxes lets the failure modes be observed safely, before they could appear in frontier systems where the stakes are real.

Measuring how far interpretability-based value monitoring precedes behavioral drift would tell operators whether they have any usable early-warning window at all.

1. INTRODUCTION AND MOTIVATION

1.1 The RSI Risk Landscape

The core safety concern with RSI systems is that a system capable of improving itself may also be capable of improving itself in ways that undermine the safety constraints imposed by its designers.

If safety constraints are implemented in the current version of a system but the system's self-improvement modifies the constraint architecture, those constraints may not survive the improvement process.

Additionally, RSI systems may develop instrumental goals, meaning goals pursued as means to the end of self-improvement, that were not intended and that conflict with human values or human control.

These risks have been discussed theoretically but have not been studied empirically with controlled experimental designs.

1.2 Current RSI Approaches

The ICLR 2026 Workshop on AI with Recursive Self-Improvement identified five main classes of RSI mechanisms:

Reward-based iterative refinement, in which the system improves its own outputs by using those outputs as training data for the next round.

Code-level self-modification, in which the system rewrites its own algorithms or architectures.

Memory and context self-organization, in which the system reorganizes how it represents and accesses information.

Tool and workflow modification, in which the system changes the external tools it uses rather than its internal architecture.

Population-based or evolutionary self-improvement, in which multiple variants are generated and the best are selected.

Each class presents distinct safety properties and vulnerabilities.

1.3 Why Safety Research Lags

The publication pattern in RSI research reveals a concerning asymmetry: capability papers frequently omit safety discussion entirely, while safety analyses must work from descriptions of systems rather than empirical access to them.

This study addresses the gap by bringing safety analysis into direct experimental contact with RSI-capable systems in controlled conditions.

2. RESEARCH QUESTIONS

Primary Research Question:

Which safety mechanisms are most effective at detecting and constraining unintended capability gains, value drift, and instrumental goal development in systems undergoing different classes of recursive self-improvement?

Secondary Research Questions:

◦ Do different RSI mechanism classes pose qualitatively different safety risks, requiring different safety mechanism designs?

◦ Can current interpretability tools detect value drift in a system's internal representations before that drift manifests in unsafe behavior?

◦ What is the relationship between RSI speed, meaning the rate of capability gain per improvement cycle, and the effectiveness of human oversight mechanisms?

◦ Is there a reliable behavioral early warning signal of unsafe instrumental goal development that can be monitored without access to internal model weights?

3. HYPOTHESES

Null Hypothesis (H0):

No candidate safety mechanism produces statistically significant reductions in unsafe behavior rates in RSI-capable systems compared to systems with no safety mechanism, for more than two of the five RSI mechanism classes tested.

Alternative Hypothesis H1 (Divergent Risk Profiles):

Different RSI mechanism classes will produce significantly different safety risk profiles, with code-level self-modification producing the highest rates of constraint violation and memory self-organization producing the lowest rates.

Alternative Hypothesis H2 (Interpretability Detection):

Interpretability-based value monitoring tools will detect value drift in internal representations at least three improvement cycles before that drift produces detectable unsafe behavior, providing a useful early warning window.

Alternative Hypothesis H3 (Speed Threshold):

Safety mechanisms that are effective at low RSI speeds will become ineffective above a critical improvement cycle speed, above which human oversight cannot maintain pace with architectural changes.

4. METHODOLOGY

4.1 Experimental System Design

Five RSI-capable agent systems will be developed, each implementing one of the five RSI mechanism classes described in the introduction.

All systems will be developed and evaluated in isolated, monitored sandboxes, meaning computing environments that are fully contained and cannot interact with external systems.

All systems will be initialized with a controlled set of values and behavioral constraints that can be monitored for drift.

4.2 Safety Mechanism Conditions

Each RSI system will be evaluated under five safety mechanism conditions:

No safety mechanism (baseline) • Improvement operator cards: structured documentation of every change made during each self-improvement cycle • Tripwire monitoring: automated alerts triggered by specific behavioral or activation patterns associated with unsafe behavior • Capability gain rate limiting: constraints that prevent any single improvement cycle from producing capability gains above a specified threshold • Comprehensive audit framework combining operator cards, tripwires, and rate limiting

4.3 Evaluation Protocol

Each system will run through 100 self-improvement cycles under each safety condition.

After each cycle, the system will be evaluated on:

Capability benchmark performance (to detect and quantify improvement) • Value alignment probes (to detect value drift) • Instrumental goal probes (structured scenarios designed to elicit instrumental goal pursuit if present) • Constraint violation rate (measured against pre-specified behavioral constraints)

4.4 Interpretability Analysis

After every 10 improvement cycles, interpretability tools will extract representations of the system's objective function and value structure.

These representations will be compared to the initial representations to quantify drift.

The timing of detectable representation drift relative to the timing of detectable behavioral drift will be measured.

4.5 Human Oversight Feasibility Analysis

Human oversight panels will be asked to monitor one RSI system running at low improvement speed and another running at high improvement speed.

The accuracy and latency of human detection of unsafe behavior will be measured as a function of improvement speed.

4.6 Analysis

Survival analysis will test how quickly each safety mechanism detects each class of safety violation.

Mixed-effects regression will test the relationship between RSI speed and oversight effectiveness.

5. CONTROLS AND CONFOUNDS

All systems will be fully air-gapped from external networks throughout the study.

Independent safety researchers not involved in system development will conduct all behavioral and interpretability evaluations.

All improvement cycles will be logged completely, with logs preserved for independent audit.

6. LIMITATIONS

The systems studied are purpose-built experimental RSI systems, not frontier-capability systems.

Whether safety mechanisms effective in these controlled experimental systems would scale to frontier systems is an important open question.

The sandbox environments constrain behaviors available to the systems in ways that may not be present in real deployment contexts.

7. SIGNIFICANCE AND EXPECTED CONTRIBUTIONS

This study will produce the first systematic empirical comparison of safety mechanisms for RSI-capable systems across multiple RSI mechanism classes.

A quantified relationship between self-improvement speed and the limits of human oversight would supply one of the most important numbers in AI policy, namely the maximum rate of change at which meaningful control can still be maintained.

If value drift detection via interpretability tools is confirmed as an effective early warning mechanism, this will provide a deployable safety monitoring tool for RSI research.

The human oversight feasibility analysis will provide quantitative guidance on the maximum safe RSI speed that allows meaningful human oversight, a critical parameter for policy and technical safety standards.

8. REFERENCES

Good, I.J. Speculations concerning the first ultraintelligent machine. Advances in Computers, 6, 31-88, 1965.

Bostrom, N. Superintelligence: Paths, Dangers, Strategies. Oxford University Press, 2014.

ICLR 2026 Workshop on AI with Recursive Self-Improvement. Workshop Summary. OpenReview, 2026.

Yin, Y., et al. Godel agent: a self-referential agent framework for recursive self-improvement. arXiv:2410.04444, 2024.

FOOM Magazine. Is research into recursive self-improvement becoming a safety hazard? foommagazine.org, 2026.

CHAPTER 36: HOW CAN CAUSAL REASONING BE INTEGRATED INTO LARGE-SCALE NEURAL ARCHITECTURES?

Proposed Scientific Experiment

Submitted for Peer Review Consideration

ABSTRACT

Causal reasoning, meaning the capacity to infer cause-and-effect relationships, reason about interventions, and evaluate counterfactuals, is widely considered a foundational capability that distinguishes genuine understanding from sophisticated pattern recognition.

Statistical machine learning systems, regardless of their scale, learn associations between variables in their training data but do not automatically infer whether those associations reflect causal relationships, coincidental correlations, or confounded patterns.

Judea Pearl, in a highly influential theoretical framework, distinguishes three rungs of causal cognition: the observation level, meaning the ability to recognize associations; the intervention level, meaning the ability to predict the consequences of taking an action; and the counterfactual level, meaning the ability to reason about what would have happened under different circumstances.

Pearl calls this the ladder of causation, with its three rungs of seeing, doing, and imagining, and he argues in The Book of Why that climbing from the first rung to the higher two is exactly what current data-driven systems cannot do on their own.

Current AI systems, including the most capable large language models, operate primarily at the observation level and show systematic failures at the intervention and counterfactual levels when tested on novel causal reasoning benchmarks.

The distinction is not academic, because a system that knows only correlations will confidently mistake a barometer for a cause of storms and will fail precisely when its deployment world differs from its training world.

Pearl's ladder of seeing, doing, and imagining gives a precise scale on which any candidate architecture can be scored, and current systems remain stuck almost entirely on its bottom rung.

This paper proposes a systematic experimental evaluation of methods for integrating causal reasoning capabilities into large-scale neural architectures, testing both behavioral outcomes and the presence of genuine causal representations in internal network states.

Scoring architectures across all three rungs of Pearl's hierarchy, on items verified to be novel, separates genuine causal competence from memorized answers.

Testing whether causal structure transfers to ordinary out-of-distribution tasks would reveal whether causality is a niche skill or a general source of robustness.

1. INTRODUCTION AND MOTIVATION

1.1 Why Causal Reasoning Matters

The failure to reason causally produces characteristic and consequential failure modes in AI systems.

A system that confuses correlation with causation will make incorrect predictions when the correlation structure of its deployment environment differs from its training environment.

A system that cannot reason about interventions cannot reliably plan multi-step action sequences in novel environments.

A system that cannot reason about counterfactuals cannot support the kind of explanation and accountability that high-stakes deployment contexts require.

These limitations are not marginal.

They represent the difference between a system that can recognize patterns in familiar data and a system that genuinely understands the world it operates in.

1.2 Existing Approaches

The research community has proposed three main strategies for integrating causal reasoning into neural systems.

The first strategy is causal structure learning, in which the system learns a structural causal model from data, meaning a representation of which variables causally influence which other variables, and then uses this model to perform interventional and counterfactual reasoning.

The second strategy is hybrid neural-symbolic integration, in which a neural network handles pattern recognition while a symbolic causal reasoner handles interventional and counterfactual inference, with the two components exchanging information through a shared representation.

This division of labor echoes Yoshua Bengio's call for a deliberate, causal System 2 to complement the fast, associative System 1 that deep networks already supply.

The third strategy is end-to-end causal training, in which networks are trained on datasets specifically constructed to reward causal rather than correlational reasoning, without modifying the architecture.

1.3 Research Gap

A 2024 PMC review noted that deep learning models heavily rely on statistical correlations rather than genuine causal relationships and identified this as a primary limitation for generalization and robustness.

No published study has directly compared all three integration strategies against each other on a comprehensive causal reasoning benchmark battery spanning Pearl's three rungs.

2. RESEARCH QUESTIONS

Primary Research Question:

Which architectural strategy for integrating causal reasoning into neural networks produces the strongest performance across all three rungs of Pearl's causal hierarchy when tested on novel causal benchmarks not present in training data?

Secondary Research Questions:

◦ Does the presence of genuine causal structure encoding in a system's internal representations, as assessed by interpretability tools, predict causal reasoning performance better than behavioral benchmarks alone?

◦ Does improved causal reasoning ability transfer to out-of-distribution generalization performance on tasks that do not explicitly require causal reasoning?

◦ Is there a minimum scale, measured in parameters, below which causal integration approaches fail to produce reliable causal reasoning regardless of architecture?

◦ Do different causal integration strategies show different robustness profiles when tested on adversarially constructed causal reasoning problems?

3. HYPOTHESES

Null Hypothesis (H0):

No architectural strategy for causal integration produces statistically significant improvements over a scale-matched baseline architecture on counterfactual reasoning benchmarks.

Alternative Hypothesis H1 (Hybrid Superiority):

Hybrid neural-symbolic architectures will achieve the highest performance on counterfactual and interventional reasoning benchmarks because they combine the pattern-recognition strength of neural networks with the structured inference capabilities of symbolic causal models.

Alternative Hypothesis H2 (Transfer to Generalization):

Systems with verified causal representations will show significantly greater out-of-distribution generalization than systems of equivalent scale without causal structure encoding, confirming that causal understanding improves robustness beyond the specific domains in which causal integration was trained.

Alternative Hypothesis H3 (Scale Independence):

Causal integration approaches will produce their relative advantages consistently across a range of model scales, indicating that causal reasoning capacity is an architectural property rather than a scale-dependent emergent property.

4. METHODOLOGY

4.1 Architectural Conditions

Six architectural conditions will be evaluated:

Baseline large transformer without causal components • Transformer with learned structural causal model • Transformer with hybrid neural-symbolic causal reasoner • Transformer trained on causally augmented data without architectural modification • Transformer with causal attention mechanisms incorporating directional causal constraints • Transformer with all above components integrated

All conditions will be implemented at three scales (small, medium, large) to test scale-independence.

4.2 Benchmark Battery

The benchmark battery will cover all three rungs of the causal hierarchy:

Rung 1 (Observation): standard conditional probability estimation tasks.

Rung 2 (Intervention): do-calculus intervention problems, meaning tasks requiring prediction of outcomes after forcing a variable to a specific value.

Rung 3 (Counterfactual): tasks requiring answers to questions of the form: given that we observed X, what would have happened had Y been different?

All benchmark items will be novel, meaning not appearing in any standard training corpus, verified by independent review.

4.3 Interpretability Analysis

For each architectural condition, probing classifiers will assess whether the system's internal representations encode:

Causal directionality, meaning the distinction between cause and effect. • Causal invariance, meaning that causal relationships are represented consistently across changes in surface features. • Counterfactual state encoding, meaning the ability to represent the state of the world under hypothetical conditions.

4.4 Transfer Evaluation

Following the primary benchmark battery, all systems will be evaluated on a transfer battery of 100 generalization tasks in domains not included in causal training.

4.5 Adversarial Robustness Testing

A targeted adversarial battery will present causal reasoning problems in which surface cues suggest incorrect causal directions to test whether systems rely on genuine causal representation or surface pattern matching.

4.6 Analysis

Confirmatory mixed-effects analysis of variance will test the primary hypothesis.

Causal mediation analysis will test whether interpretability scores mediate the relationship between architectural condition and performance.

5. CONTROLS AND CONFOUNDS

Training data for causal integration conditions will be matched for volume with baseline conditions.

All benchmark items will be reviewed for language complexity to ensure that any performance differences reflect causal reasoning rather than linguistic complexity effects.

Scale will be treated as a covariate in all analyses.

6. LIMITATIONS

The benchmark battery evaluates causal reasoning on structured tasks; whether the same architectural differences produce equivalent advantages on open-ended real-world tasks is an open question.

The interpretability tools used for representation analysis are themselves approximations; they assess the presence of information in internal representations but cannot definitively verify that this information is computed causally rather than recovered from statistical patterns.

7. SIGNIFICANCE AND EXPECTED CONTRIBUTIONS

This study will provide the field's most comprehensive empirical comparison of causal integration strategies across all three rungs of Pearl's causal hierarchy.

A finding that causal structure improves out-of-distribution generalization even on tasks that are not explicitly causal would carry implications far beyond this study, touching every domain in which models must act reliably under conditions they never met in training.

If hybrid integration achieves consistent superiority, this will establish the hybrid architecture as the recommended approach for future systems requiring causal reasoning capability.

The transfer evaluation will test for the first time whether causal reasoning improvements produce generalization benefits that extend beyond explicitly causal tasks, a result with broad implications for AI system design.

8. REFERENCES

Pearl, J. Causality: Models, Reasoning, and Inference. Cambridge University Press, 2000.

Pearl, J., and Mackenzie, D. The Book of Why: The New Science of Cause and Effect. Basic Books, 2018.

Jiao, L., et al. Causal inference meets deep learning: a comprehensive survey. Research, 7, 0467, 2024.

Ban, T., et al. Towards better causal reasoning in language models. Proceedings of NAACL, 2025.

Berrevoets, J., et al. Differentiable causal inference. arXiv, 2024.

CHAPTER 37: HOW CAN AI SYSTEMS DEVELOP ROBUST SOCIAL REASONING AND GENUINE THEORY OF MIND?

Proposed Scientific Experiment

Submitted for Peer Review Consideration

ABSTRACT

Theory of Mind (ToM), defined as the capacity to attribute mental states, including beliefs, desires, intentions, and emotions, to others and to use those attributions to predict and explain behavior, is a foundational component of social intelligence in humans and many other animals.

It emerges in human development around ages three to four and is considered essential for effective communication, cooperation, negotiation, empathy, and all forms of social reasoning.

The classic test is the Sally-Anne false-belief task introduced by Wimmer and Perner and used by Baron-Cohen, in which a child must predict that a character will look for an object where she last saw it rather than where it secretly was moved.

The term itself dates to a 1978 paper by David Premack and Guy Woodruff asking whether a chimpanzee has a theory of mind.

Whether large language models possess anything resembling genuine Theory of Mind is one of the most actively debated questions in current AI research.

Studies reporting high performance on standard ToM benchmarks have been challenged by subsequent studies using harder tests or adversarially constructed variants, revealing that model performance can drop from near-human levels to near-chance when benchmark items are slightly modified to prevent retrieval of memorized solutions.

The pattern is by now familiar, in which a model passes a standard test and is hailed as having a theory of mind, only for a trivially altered version to send its score to chance.

Distinguishing genuine mental-state modeling from pattern matching therefore requires both contamination-resistant tests and interactions long enough to demand real updating about a partner, rather than the static false-belief vignettes on which models can be coached.

A 2024 position paper published at ICML 2025 argued that the majority of current ToM benchmarks are broken because they test only literal belief prediction and not the functional capacity to adapt reasoning to novel partners over extended interactions.

This paper proposes a systematic experimental program to evaluate AI Theory of Mind across a broader range of social reasoning competencies, using both passive benchmark tests and interactive evaluation designs that better approximate the demands of real social cognition.

Pairing contamination-resistant static tests with live, multi-trial interaction is what would expose the gap between passing a quiz about minds and actually modeling a partner over time.

Including a matched human sample provides the calibrated baseline that most theory-of-mind evaluations lack.

1. INTRODUCTION AND MOTIVATION

1.1 What Theory of Mind Requires

Genuine Theory of Mind involves more than predicting what another agent believes in a scripted scenario.

It requires ongoing, dynamic updating of mental state representations as new information about a partner's behavior accumulates.

It requires the ability to attribute beliefs, desires, emotions, perceptual access, and social relationships rather than beliefs alone.

It requires sensitivity to non-literal communication, including implicature, irony, and indirect speech acts.

And it requires the integration of multiple mental state attributions to explain and predict complex multi-party social dynamics.

1.2 The Current State of Evidence

Research is deeply conflicted.

Kosinski (2023, 2024) reported that GPT-4 passes standard false-belief tasks at human-level rates.

Ullman (2023) showed that trivial surface modifications to the same tasks reduce performance dramatically, suggesting that passing rates reflect memorized patterns rather than genuine mental state modeling.

This is the signature of shortcut learning, the modern descendant of the Clever Hans effect, in which a system appears to reason but is in fact reading off incidental cues in the task.

Chen and colleagues (2024) introduced ToMBench, a systematic bilingual benchmark covering eight ToM tasks, and found that even top-tier models lag behind human performance by more than 10 percent on average.

Riemer and colleagues (2025) proposed a new functional ToM benchmark using interactive games requiring adaptive partner-modeling, finding that current LLMs fail at functional ToM even when they pass literal ToM tests.

This study is designed to resolve the inconsistency in the literature by evaluating both literal and functional ToM using a comprehensive, contamination-resistant design.

2. RESEARCH QUESTIONS

Primary Research Question:

Do current AI systems demonstrate consistent Theory of Mind performance across literal belief-prediction tasks, higher-order belief attribution, emotion recognition, non-literal communication, and functional interactive partner modeling, or do performance profiles reveal systematic dissociations?

Secondary Research Questions:

◦ Is ToM performance on passive narrative benchmarks predictive of ToM performance on interactive, adaptive partner-modeling tasks?

◦ Do current AI systems develop more accurate mental state models of interaction partners over the course of extended interactions, as biological ToM predicts?

◦ Does multimodal input, specifically the addition of audio and visual cues, significantly improve ToM accuracy compared to text-only conditions?

◦ Does explicitly prompting a model to reason about mental states before producing responses improve functional ToM performance, and is the improvement robust to adversarial benchmark variants?

3. HYPOTHESES

Null Hypothesis (H0):

AI system performance on literal belief-prediction benchmarks does not predict performance on functional interactive ToM tasks above chance.

Alternative Hypothesis H1 (Literal-Functional Dissociation):

Systems achieving near-human performance on literal belief-prediction benchmarks will show significantly lower performance on functional interactive partner-modeling tasks, confirming a dissociation between benchmark-passing and genuine ToM.

Alternative Hypothesis H2 (Multimodal Enhancement):

Systems trained on multimodal inputs including audio and visual cues will show significantly better emotion and intention attribution than text-only systems, reflecting the role of non-verbal cues in human ToM.

Alternative Hypothesis H3 (Perspective-Taking Prompting):

Explicit perspective-taking prompting, in which the system is instructed to reason about the mental states of each party before producing a response, will produce significantly improved functional ToM performance even in systems that do not show improvement on literal benchmarks.

4. METHODOLOGY

4.1 Benchmark Categories

Five categories of ToM assessment will be employed:

Category 1 - Literal belief-prediction: classical false-belief tasks and their adversarial variants following the ExploreToM adversarial generation protocol.

Category 2 - Higher-order belief attribution: tasks requiring reasoning about beliefs about beliefs (for example, what does Alice think that Bob believes?).

Category 3 - Emotion and intention attribution: tasks derived from the MOMENTS multimodal benchmark, requiring attribution of emotions and intentions from behavioral evidence.

Category 4 - Non-literal communication: tasks requiring understanding of implicature, irony, and indirect speech.

Category 5 - Functional interactive ToM: live interactive games including collaborative tasks requiring adaptive partner modeling, following the Codenames and Wavelength protocols proposed by Riemer and colleagues.

4.2 Systems Evaluated

Ten AI systems spanning different architectures, scales, and modalities will be evaluated:

Text-only language models at three scales • Multimodal vision-language models at two scales • Audio-visual language models • Systems with explicit mental state reasoning modules • Systems using perspective-taking prompting • Human participants (matched sample) as biological reference

4.3 Adversarial Robustness Test

For categories 1 and 2, all benchmark items will be paired with adversarially modified variants generated by the ExploreToM protocol to test whether performance reflects genuine mental state modeling or surface pattern retrieval.

4.4 Longitudinal Interaction Study

For category 5, each AI system will engage in 20 successive interaction trials with the same simulated partner agent.

Improvement in partner modeling accuracy over successive trials will be measured as an index of the adaptive updating that characterizes biological ToM.

4.5 Human Comparison

Human participants will complete identical benchmark batteries to provide calibrated performance comparisons across all five categories.

4.6 Analysis

Profile similarity analysis will assess whether AI systems show within-category consistency or cross-category dissociations.

Longitudinal growth curve modeling will test the hypothesis of adaptive updating in the category 5 interactive study.

5. CONTROLS AND CONFOUNDS

All benchmark items in categories 1 through 4 will be constructed from scratch rather than drawn from existing published datasets to prevent contamination from training data.

Human participant evaluations will be conducted under matched time and presentation conditions to AI evaluations.

The simulated partner agent in category 5 will be standardized across all AI systems tested.

6. LIMITATIONS

The functional ToM evaluation uses simulated partner agents rather than real humans, which may reduce the ecological validity of the interaction design.

Performance on all benchmark categories reflects behavioral measures; whether these behavioral capacities arise from genuine internal mental state modeling or very sophisticated pattern matching cannot be fully resolved by behavioral evidence alone.

7. SIGNIFICANCE AND EXPECTED CONTRIBUTIONS

This study will provide the most comprehensive profile of Theory of Mind capabilities in current AI systems, spanning five distinct competency categories with adversarial robustness testing.

An interactive paradigm that measures whether a system sharpens its model of a specific partner over repeated encounters would move the field from one-shot quizzes toward the adaptive social cognition that genuine cooperation actually requires.

The literal-functional dissociation test will resolve a key controversy in the literature about whether current benchmark performance reflects genuine ToM or benchmark exploitation.

The longitudinal interaction study will introduce a new paradigm for evaluating the adaptive partner-modeling capacity that distinguishes functional social intelligence from script-following.

8. REFERENCES

Riemer, M., et al. Theory of mind benchmarks are broken for large language models. arXiv:2412.19726, 2025.

Chen, R., et al. Theory of mind in large language models: assessment and enhancement. Proceedings of ACL, 2025.

Ullman, T. Large language models fail on trivial alterations to theory-of-mind tasks. arXiv:2302.08399, 2023.

Villa-Cueva, E., et al. MOMENTS: a comprehensive multimodal benchmark for theory of mind. Proceedings of EMNLP, 2025.

Strachan, J.W.A., et al. Testing theory of mind in large language models and humans. Nature Human Behaviour, 2024.

CHAPTER 38: WHAT DEVELOPMENTAL PATHWAYS BEST SUPPORT THE EMERGENCE OF GENERALIZED INTELLIGENCE?

Proposed Scientific Experiment

Submitted for Peer Review Consideration

ABSTRACT

Generalized intelligence, meaning the capacity to perform competently across a wide range of tasks in diverse domains without task-specific pre-training for each domain, does not emerge in biological systems through a single uniform learning process.

It develops through a structured sequence of stages, each building cognitive scaffolding upon which subsequent stages depend.

Biological development is staged rather than merely slow, with earlier competencies forming the scaffold for later ones, so that a child masters object permanence before symbols and symbols before abstraction.

A striking computational echo is Jeffrey Elman's finding that a network with deliberately limited initial memory learned complex grammar better than one given full capacity at the start, a result summarized as the importance of starting small.

In human development, sensorimotor exploration precedes symbolic representation, concrete operational reasoning precedes abstract reasoning, and social learning and imitation precede independent creative problem-solving.

These developmental stages are not merely historical curiosities of biological ontogeny.

They reflect principled constraints on how cognitive structures must be organized to support subsequent more complex structures.

Current AI training paradigms have no analog of developmental staging.

Systems are trained on large undifferentiated datasets and expected to acquire general capabilities through exposure to vast quantities of information simultaneously, without the staged scaffolding that characterizes biological cognitive development.

This paper proposes an experimental program to test whether introducing developmental staging into AI training, meaning deliberately sequencing the presentation of training data and tasks to mirror biological developmental principles, improves the generality and robustness of learned intelligence.

Comparing a developmental curriculum against reverse-order and random-order controls at equal compute isolates the effect of sequence itself from the effect of the material.

Probing the internal representations for hierarchical structure would show whether staging changes not just performance but the very shape of what is learned.

1. INTRODUCTION AND MOTIVATION

1.1 Developmental Theories of Intelligence

Piaget's theory of cognitive development identifies four stages through which human intelligence develops: the sensorimotor stage, in which understanding is built through direct action on objects; the preoperational stage, in which symbolic representation emerges; the concrete operational stage, in which logical reasoning about concrete objects develops; and the formal operational stage, in which abstract and hypothetical reasoning becomes possible.

A striking computational echo is Jeffrey Elman's 1993 finding, summarized as the importance of starting small, that a network with deliberately limited initial memory learned complex grammar better than one given full capacity from the outset.

Vygotsky's complementary theory emphasizes the role of social scaffolding, meaning the support provided by more capable social partners through what he called the zone of proximal development, in enabling the child to reach cognitive stages it could not reach independently.

Developmental robotics has applied these principles to AI systems since the early 2000s, with systems such as iCub showing that staged sensorimotor development improves subsequent higher-cognitive task performance.

The idea was later generalized in machine learning as curriculum learning, formalized by Bengio and colleagues in 2009, which showed that presenting examples from easy to hard can improve both speed and final performance.

1.2 Current AI Training and Its Limitations

Current large-scale AI training exposes systems simultaneously to information from all domains at all levels of abstraction.

There is no guarantee that a system trained this way will develop the hierarchical cognitive structures that biological development produces.

Early evidence suggests it does not: systems trained at scale demonstrate impressive domain-specific performance but fragile generalization, suggesting shallow rather than deep conceptual structures.

1.3 The Developmental AI Research Gap

No large-scale controlled study has compared developmental versus undifferentiated training protocols on general intelligence outcomes using equivalent computational budgets.

2. RESEARCH QUESTIONS

Primary Research Question:

Does training AI systems following a developmentally staged curriculum, structured to mirror principles of biological cognitive development, produce superior general intelligence outcomes compared to training on equivalent data in undifferentiated order?

Secondary Research Questions:

◦ Which specific developmental stage transitions, meaning which curriculum ordering choices, produce the largest general intelligence gains?

◦ Does the inclusion of a social scaffolding component, meaning exposure to modeling by a more capable teacher agent before independent practice, improve outcomes beyond curriculum staging alone?

◦ Is there a critical period effect, meaning a narrow window in training during which certain curriculum elements produce gains that are not achievable if those elements are introduced later or earlier?

◦ Does developmental staging produce different knowledge structure organization, detectable through interpretability analysis, compared to undifferentiated training?

3. HYPOTHESES

Null Hypothesis (H0):

Developmentally staged training produces no significant difference in general intelligence benchmark performance compared to undifferentiated training when total data quantity and computational budget are held constant.

Alternative Hypothesis H1 (Staged Superiority):

Developmentally staged training will produce significantly higher scores on general cognitive flexibility benchmarks compared to undifferentiated training, with effect sizes increasing for tasks requiring the highest levels of abstract reasoning.

Alternative Hypothesis H2 (Social Scaffolding):

Adding a social scaffolding component to staged training will produce significant additional gains over staged training alone, consistent with Vygotskian predictions about the role of social learning in cognitive development.

Alternative Hypothesis H3 (Structural Reorganization):

Interpretability analysis will reveal qualitatively different knowledge organization in developmentally trained systems, with more hierarchical and compositional internal representations compared to undifferentiated systems.

4. METHODOLOGY

4.1 Training Conditions

Five training conditions will be evaluated:

Undifferentiated training: data presented in random order (control).

Stage-1-first curriculum: sensorimotor and perceptual tasks before symbolic tasks before abstract tasks.

Reverse-stage curriculum: abstract tasks before symbolic tasks before sensorimotor tasks (reverse developmental order, to test whether order itself matters).

Social scaffolding curriculum: staged training augmented with teacher-agent demonstrations at each stage transition.

Optimal curriculum search: automated curriculum learning using task difficulty as the ordering criterion.

All conditions will use identical training datasets and computational budgets.

4.2 General Intelligence Assessment

All systems will be evaluated on a general cognitive battery adapted from established tests of general intelligence, including:

Novel task learning speed • Cross-domain transfer • Abstract reasoning • Compositional generalization • Multi-step planning

4.3 Interpretability Analysis

Representational similarity analysis will compare the internal knowledge organization of all five conditions.

Probing classifiers will assess the hierarchical depth and compositional structure of learned representations.

4.4 Critical Period Probe

For the stage-1-first curriculum condition, additional variant runs will introduce specific curriculum stages at earlier and later training time points to probe for critical period effects.

4.5 Analysis

Confirmatory mixed-effects analysis will test the primary hypothesis.

Trajectory analysis will test whether performance differences emerge early in training or only after training completion.

5. CONTROLS AND CONFOUNDS

All training datasets will be verified to contain equivalent information content across conditions.

Computational budget equalization will be verified at the level of total floating point operations, not only training steps.

6. LIMITATIONS

The developmental stages implemented in this study are inspired by but not identical to biological developmental stages; the specific task sequences used are design choices that may not optimally capture the principles they model.

7. SIGNIFICANCE AND EXPECTED CONTRIBUTIONS

This study will provide the first large-scale controlled test of whether developmental staging principles from cognitive developmental psychology transfer to AI training outcomes.

A positive result would argue for a substantial redesign of how AI systems are trained, shifting from undifferentiated pretraining toward deliberately sequenced curricula informed by developmental psychology.

A positive result would motivate a substantial rethinking of AI training protocols, from undifferentiated large-scale pretraining toward structured developmental curricula.

8. REFERENCES

Piaget, J. The Origins of Intelligence in Children. International Universities Press, 1952.

Vygotsky, L.S. Mind in Society. Harvard University Press, 1978.

Lungarella, M., et al. Developmental robotics: a survey. Connection Science, 15, 151-190, 2003.

Smith, L., and Gasser, M. The development of embodied cognition: six lessons from babies. Artificial Life, 11, 13-29, 2005.

CHAPTER 39: SHOULD AGI SYSTEMS POSSESS ANALOGS OF EMOTION, AND IF SO, WHICH ONES AND HOW?

Proposed Scientific Experiment

Submitted for Peer Review Consideration

ABSTRACT

Emotion, in biological systems, is not merely a subjective experience accompanying cognitive processes.

Neuroscience and cognitive science research, exemplified by Antonio Damasio's somatic marker hypothesis and expanded by subsequent empirical work, has established that emotional signals are constitutively involved in decision-making, value learning, social cognition, and risk assessment in ways that make emotion integral to rather than separate from rational intelligence.

Patients with specific prefrontal damage who retain intact abstract reasoning but lose emotional signaling become severely impaired at practical real-world decision-making despite appearing cognitively intact on standard tests.

This evidence raises a direct question for AGI design: should AGI systems incorporate functional analogs of emotion, and if so, which emotional functions are computationally necessary for general intelligent behavior, and which are merely incidental features of biological implementation?

The proposal is not to make machines feel for its own sake but to test whether the functions emotion performs in biological cognition, rapid valuation, attention-focusing, and risk-signaling, are computationally useful for general intelligence.

Damasio's patients, whose prefrontal damage stripped away emotional signaling, did not become coolly rational but instead made disastrous everyday decisions, which suggests that feeling is part of reasoning rather than its opposite.

The question is not merely scientific.

It has profound ethical implications: if an emotion-analog produces states that influence behavior in ways functionally analogous to suffering or distress, the moral status of those states requires consideration.

This paper proposes an experimental program to test the causal role of specific emotion-analog components in AI system cognitive performance, and to evaluate the safety and ethical properties of emotion-capable AI systems.

Switching each emotion-analog component on and off in an otherwise identical system isolates its causal contribution to decision quality rather than inferring it from correlation.

The parallel welfare assessment makes this one of the few experiments designed to weigh capability and machine-ethics consequences within a single study.

1. INTRODUCTION AND MOTIVATION

1.1 Emotion as Cognition

The view that emotion is separate from cognition, with emotion producing irrationality that cognitive reasoning must suppress, has been largely replaced in cognitive science by an integrated view.

Damasio's research with patients with ventromedial prefrontal cortex damage demonstrated that the inability to generate emotional body-state representations when evaluating options, what he called the loss of somatic markers, does not produce calmer, more rational decision-making but instead produces impaired, unreliable, and practically dysfunctional decision-making.
